<p><b>Science fiction</b> (often shortened to <b>sci-fi</b> or <b>scifi</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> of <a href="/wiki/Speculative_fiction" title="Speculative fiction">speculative fiction</a>, typically dealing with <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imaginative</a> concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">futuristic</a> <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">space travel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Time_travel_in_fiction" title="Time travel in fiction">time travel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faster_than_light_travel" title="Faster than light travel">faster than light travel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parallel_universe_(fiction)" title="Parallel universe (fiction)">parallel universes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>. Science fiction often explores the potential consequences of scientific and other <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovations</a>, and has been called a "literature of ideas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Fiction:_The_Literature_of_Ideas_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Fiction:_The_Literature_of_Ideas-1">[1]</a></sup> It usually eschews the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, and unlike the related genre of <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a>, historically science fiction stories were intended to have at least a faint grounding in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science-based fact or theory</a> at the time the story was created, but this connection has become tenuous or non-existent in much of science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_science_fiction" title="Definitions of science fiction">Definitions of science fiction</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:257px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="3200" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png/255px-Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png/383px-Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png/510px-Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png 2x" width="255"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genghis-jones-pod-active_mango_concept-art_02.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A person reads from a futuristic wraparound display screen.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Science fiction is difficult to define, as it includes a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subgenre" title="Subgenre">subgenres</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">themes</a>. Author and editor <a href="/wiki/Damon_Knight" title="Damon Knight">Damon Knight</a> summed up the difficulty, saying "science fiction is what we point to when we say it",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-In_Search_of_Wonder:_Essays_on_Modern_Science_Fiction_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-In_Search_of_Wonder:_Essays_on_Modern_Science_Fiction-3">[3]</a></sup> a definition echoed by author Mark C. Glassy, who argues that the definition of science fiction is like the definition of <a href="/wiki/Pornography" title="Pornography">pornography</a>: you do not know what it is, but you know it when you see it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Biology_of_Science_Fiction_Cinema_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Biology_of_Science_Fiction_Cinema-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hugo_Gernsback" title="Hugo Gernsback">Hugo Gernsback</a>, who was one of the first in using the term "science fiction", described his vision of the genre: "By 'scientifiction' I mean the <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>, <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a> type of story—a charming romance intermingled with scientific fact and prophetic vision."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1970 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Atheling_Jr." title="William Atheling Jr.">William Atheling Jr.</a> (James Blish) wrote about the English term "science fiction": "Wells used the term originally to cover what we would today call ‘hard’ science fiction, in which a conscientious attempt to be faithful to already known facts (as of the date of writing) was the substrate on which the story was to be built, and if the story was also to contain a miracle, it ought at least not to contain a whole arsenal of them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Heinlein" title="Robert A. Heinlein">Robert A. Heinlein</a>, "a handy short definition of almost all science fiction might read: realistic speculation about possible future events, based solidly on adequate knowledge of the real world, past and present, and on a thorough understanding of the nature and significance of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heinlein_def_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-heinlein_def-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rod_Serling" title="Rod Serling">Rod Serling</a>'s definition is "fantasy is the impossible made probable. Science fiction is the improbable made possible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serling_def_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serling_def-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lester_del_Rey" title="Lester del Rey">Lester del Rey</a> wrote, "Even the devoted aficionado—or fan—has a hard time trying to explain what science fiction is", and that the reason for there not being a "full satisfactory definition" is that "there are no easily delineated limits to science fiction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_of_Science_Fiction_1926.E2.80.931976_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_of_Science_Fiction_1926.E2.80.931976-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Science fiction is largely based on writing <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationally</a> about alternative possible worlds or futures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_of_Science_Fiction:_1926.E2.80.931976_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_of_Science_Fiction:_1926.E2.80.931976-10">[10]</a></sup> It is related to, but different from <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> in that, within the context of the <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">story</a>, its imaginary elements are largely possible within <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientifically</a> established or scientifically postulated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_laws" title="Physical laws">physical laws</a> (though some elements in a story might still be pure imaginative speculation).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Setting_(narrative)" title="Setting (narrative)">settings</a> of science fiction are often contrary to those of <a href="/wiki/Consensus_reality" title="Consensus reality">consensus reality</a>, but most science fiction relies on a considerable degree of <a href="/wiki/Suspension_of_disbelief" title="Suspension of disbelief">suspension of disbelief</a>, which is facilitated in the reader's mind by potential scientific explanations or solutions to various fictional elements. Science fiction elements include:</p>
<ul>
<li>A time setting in the <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">future</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">alternative timelines</a>, or in a historical past that contradicts known facts of <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> record.</li>
<li>A spatial setting or scenes in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a>), on other worlds, or on <a href="/wiki/Subterranea_(geography)" title="Subterranea (geography)">subterranean earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>Characters that include <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">aliens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutant_(fictional)" title="Mutant (fictional)">mutants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Android_(robot)" title="Android (robot)">androids</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Humanoid" title="Humanoid">humanoid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robots" title="Robots">robots</a> and other types of characters arising from a future human evolution.</li>
<li>Futuristic or plausible technology such as <a href="/wiki/Raygun" title="Raygun">ray guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teleportation" title="Teleportation">teleportation</a> machines, and humanoid <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_to_Write_Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-How_to_Write_Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy-12">[12]</a></sup></li>
<li>Scientific principles that are new or that contradict accepted physical laws, for example <a href="/wiki/Time_travel" title="Time travel">time travel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wormhole" title="Wormhole">wormholes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Faster-than-light" title="Faster-than-light">faster-than-light</a> travel or <a href="/wiki/Ansible" title="Ansible">communication</a>.</li>
<li>New and different political or social systems, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopian" title="Utopian">utopian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-scarcity" title="Post-scarcity">post-scarcity</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-apocalyptic" title="Post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Age_of_Wonders:_Exploring_the_World_of_Science_Fiction_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Age_of_Wonders:_Exploring_the_World_of_Science_Fiction-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paranormal" title="Paranormal">Paranormal</a> abilities such as <a href="/wiki/Mind_control" title="Mind control">mind control</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telepathy" title="Telepathy">telepathy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telekinesis" title="Telekinesis">telekinesis</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/The_Force_(Star_Wars)" title="The Force (Star Wars)">"The Force"</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>Other universes or <a href="/wiki/Parallel_universe_(fiction)#Science_fiction" title="Parallel universe (fiction)">dimensions</a> and travel between them.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_fiction" title="History of science fiction">History of science fiction</a>.</div>
<p>As a means of understanding the world through speculation and storytelling, science fiction has antecedents which go back to an era when the dividing line separating the mythological from the historical tends to become somewhat blurred, though precursors to science fiction as literature can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">Lucian</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/True_History" title="True History">True History</a></i> in the 2nd century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> some of the <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">Arabian Nights</a></i> tales,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Arabian_Nights:_A_Companion_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Arabian_Nights:_A_Companion-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson-17">[17]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i> in the 10th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson-17">[17]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theologus_Autodidactus" title="Theologus Autodidactus">Theologus Autodidactus</a></i> in the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roubi_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roubi-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>A product of the budding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Reason" title="Age of Reason">Age of Reason</a> and the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> itself, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Somnium_(novel)" title="Somnium (novel)">Somnium</a></i> (1620–1630).,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Harmony_of_the_Worlds_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Harmony_of_the_Worlds-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyrano_de_Bergerac" title="Cyrano de Bergerac">Cyrano de Bergerac</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Comical_History_of_the_States_and_Empires_of_the_Moon" title="Comical History of the States and Empires of the Moon">Comical History of the States and Empires of the Moon</a></i> (1657), his <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_States_and_Empires_of_the_Sun&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The States and Empires of the Sun (page does not exist)">The States and Empires of the Sun</a></i> (1662), <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Cavendish,_Duchess_of_Newcastle-upon-Tyne" title="Margaret Cavendish, Duchess of Newcastle-upon-Tyne">Margaret Cavendish</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/The_Blazing_World" title="The Blazing World">The Blazing World</a>" (1666),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver's Travels">Gulliver's Travels</a></i> (1726), <a href="/wiki/Ludvig_Holberg" title="Ludvig Holberg">Ludvig Holberg</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Niels_Klim%27s_Underground_Travels" title="Niels Klim's Underground Travels">Nicolai Klimii Iter Subterraneum</a></i> (1741) and <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Microm%C3%A9gas" title="Micromégas">Micromégas</a></i> (1752) are some of the first true science fantasy works,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_cites_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica_cites-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> which often feature the adventures of the protagonist in fictional and fantastical places, or the moon. <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> considered Kepler's work the first science fiction story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It depicts a journey to the Moon and how the Earth's motion is seen from there.</p>
<p>Following the 18th-century development of the <a href="/wiki/Novel" title="Novel">novel</a> as a literary form, in the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s books <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> (1818) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Man" title="The Last Man">The Last Man</a></i> (1826) helped define the form of the science fiction novel, and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Aldiss" title="Brian Aldiss">Brian Aldiss</a> has argued that <i>Frankenstein</i> was the first work of science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a> wrote a story about a flight to the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poe_moon_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-poe_moon-25">[25]</a></sup> More examples appeared throughout the 19th century.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg"><img alt="Black-and-white photo of a man with bushy black mustache and black hair with parting." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="443" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg/170px-H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg/255px-H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg/340px-H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H_G_Wells_pre_1922.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Then with the dawn of new technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegraph" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>, and new forms of powered transportation, writers including <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a> created a body of work that became popular across broad cross-sections of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verne_wells_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-verne_wells-26">[26]</a></sup> Wells' <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> (1898) describes an invasion of late Victorian England by Martians using tripod fighting machines equipped with advanced weaponry. It is a seminal depiction of an <a href="/wiki/Alien_invasion" title="Alien invasion">alien invasion</a> of Earth.</p>
<p>In the late 19th century, the term "<a href="/wiki/Scientific_romance" title="Scientific romance">scientific romance</a>" was used in Britain to describe much of this fiction. This produced additional offshoots, such as the 1884 novella <i><a href="/wiki/Flatland" title="Flatland">Flatland: A Romance of Many Dimensions</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Abbott_Abbott" title="Edwin Abbott Abbott">Edwin Abbott Abbott</a>. The term would continue to be used into the early 20th century for writers such as <a href="/wiki/Olaf_Stapledon" title="Olaf Stapledon">Olaf Stapledon</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_(restoration).jpg"><img alt="Black-and-white photo of man in formal dress with unkempt hair, mustache and beard." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2111" data-file-width="1508" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_%28restoration%29.jpg/170px-F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_%28restoration%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_%28restoration%29.jpg/255px-F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_%28restoration%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_%28restoration%29.jpg/340px-F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_%28restoration%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A9lix_Nadar_1820-1910_portraits_Jules_Verne_(restoration).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp magazines</a> helped develop a new generation of mainly American SF writers, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Gernsback" title="Hugo Gernsback">Hugo Gernsback</a>, the founder of <i><a href="/wiki/Amazing_Stories" title="Amazing Stories">Amazing Stories</a></i> magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sf_history_nvcc_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-sf_history_nvcc-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1912 <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Rice_Burroughs" title="Edgar Rice Burroughs">Edgar Rice Burroughs</a> published <i><a href="/wiki/A_Princess_of_Mars" title="A Princess of Mars">A Princess of Mars</a></i>, the first of his three-decade-long series of <a href="/wiki/Barsoom" title="Barsoom">Barsoom</a> novels, situated on Mars and featuring <a href="/wiki/John_Carter_of_Mars" title="John Carter of Mars">John Carter</a> as the hero. The 1928 publication of <a href="/wiki/Philip_Francis_Nowlan" title="Philip Francis Nowlan">Philip Francis Nowlan</a>'s original <a href="/wiki/Buck_Rogers" title="Buck Rogers">Buck Rogers</a> story, <i>Armageddon 2419</i>, in <i>Amazing Stories</i> was a landmark event. This story led to comic strips featuring Buck Rogers (1929), <i><a href="/wiki/Brick_Bradford" title="Brick Bradford">Brick Bradford</a></i> (1933), and <i><a href="/wiki/Flash_Gordon" title="Flash Gordon">Flash Gordon</a></i> (1934). The comic strips and derivative movie serials greatly popularized science fiction.</p>
<p>In the late 1930s, <a href="/wiki/John_W._Campbell" title="John W. Campbell">John W. Campbell</a> became editor of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astounding_Science_Fiction" title="Astounding Science Fiction">Astounding Science Fiction</a></i>, and a critical mass of new writers emerged in New York City in a group called the <a href="/wiki/Futurians" title="Futurians">Futurians</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damon_Knight" title="Damon Knight">Damon Knight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donald_A._Wollheim" title="Donald A. Wollheim">Donald A. Wollheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederik_Pohl" title="Frederik Pohl">Frederik Pohl</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Blish" title="James Blish">James Blish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judith_Merril" title="Judith Merril">Judith Merril</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futurians_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-futurians-28">[28]</a></sup> Other important writers during this period include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.E._Smith" title="E.E. Smith">E.E. (Doc) Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Heinlein" title="Robert A. Heinlein">Robert A. Heinlein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olaf_Stapledon" title="Olaf Stapledon">Olaf Stapledon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/A._E._van_Vogt" title="A. E. van Vogt">A. E. van Vogt</a>. Working outside the Campbell influence were <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Lem" title="Stanisław Lem">Stanisław Lem</a>. Campbell's tenure at <i>Astounding</i> is considered to be the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_science_fiction" title="Golden Age of science fiction">Golden Age of science fiction</a>, characterized by hard SF stories celebrating scientific achievement and progress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sf_history_nvcc_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-sf_history_nvcc-27">[27]</a></sup> This lasted until post-war technological advances, new magazines such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_(magazine)" title="Galaxy (magazine)">Galaxy</a></i>, edited by H. L. Gold, and a new generation of writers began writing stories with less emphasis on the hard sciences and more on the social sciences.</p>
<p>In the 1950s, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beat_generation" title="Beat generation">Beat generation</a> included speculative writers such as <a href="/wiki/William_S._Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs">William S. Burroughs</a>. In the 1960s and early 1970s, writers like <a href="/wiki/Frank_Herbert" title="Frank Herbert">Frank Herbert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_R._Delany" title="Samuel R. Delany">Samuel R. Delany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Zelazny" title="Roger Zelazny">Roger Zelazny</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Ellison" title="Harlan Ellison">Harlan Ellison</a> explored new trends, ideas, and writing styles, while a group of writers, mainly in Britain, became known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Wave_(science_fiction)" title="New Wave (science fiction)">New Wave</a> for their embrace of a high degree of experimentation, both in form and in content, and a highbrow and self-consciously "literary" or artistic sensibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_cites_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica_cites-21">[21]</a></sup> In the 1970s, writers like <a href="/wiki/Larry_Niven" title="Larry Niven">Larry Niven</a> brought new life to hard science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hard_sf_def_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-hard_sf_def-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a> and others pioneered soft science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soft_sf_period_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-soft_sf_period-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk" title="Cyberpunk">cyberpunk</a> authors like <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson" title="William Gibson">William Gibson</a> turned away from the <a href="/wiki/Optimism" title="Optimism">optimism</a> and support for progress of traditional science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gibson_cyber_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-gibson_cyber-31">[31]</a></sup> This dystopian vision of the near future is described in the work of <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Do_Androids_Dream_of_Electric_Sheep%3F" title="Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?">Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/We_Can_Remember_It_for_You_Wholesale" title="We Can Remember It for You Wholesale">We Can Remember It for You Wholesale</a></i>, which resulted in the films <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner" title="Blade Runner">Blade Runner</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Total_Recall_(1990_film)" title="Total Recall (1990 film)">Total Recall</a></i>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i> franchise helped spark a new interest in <a href="/wiki/Space_opera" title="Space opera">space opera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_opera_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-space_opera-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C._J._Cherryh" title="C. J. Cherryh">C. J. Cherryh</a>'s detailed explorations of <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">alien</a> life and complex scientific challenges influenced a generation of writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cherryh_nazarian_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-cherryh_nazarian-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emerging themes in the 1990s included <a href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_issues" title="List of environmental issues">environmental issues</a>, the implications of the global Internet and the expanding information universe, questions about <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>, as well as a post-<a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-scarcity" title="Post-scarcity">post-scarcity</a> societies; <a href="/wiki/Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson">Neal Stephenson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Diamond_Age" title="The Diamond Age">The Diamond Age</a></i> comprehensively explores these themes. <a href="/wiki/Lois_McMaster_Bujold" title="Lois McMaster Bujold">Lois McMaster Bujold</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Vorkosigan_Saga" title="Vorkosigan Saga">Vorkosigan</a></i> novels brought the character-driven story back into prominence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bujold_char_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-bujold_char-34">[34]</a></sup> The television series <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Next_Generation" title="Star Trek: The Next Generation">Star Trek: The Next Generation</a></i> (1987) began a torrent of new SF shows, including three further <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a></i> continuation shows (<a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Deep_Space_Nine" title="Star Trek: Deep Space Nine">Deep Space 9</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Voyager" title="Star Trek: Voyager">Voyager</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_Enterprise" title="Star Trek: Enterprise">Enterprise</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Babylon_5" title="Babylon 5">Babylon 5</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate_(film)" title="Stargate (film)">Stargate</a></i>, a movie about an ancient portal to other gates across the galaxy, was released in 1994. <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate_SG-1" title="Stargate SG-1">Stargate SG-1</a></i>, a TV series, premiered on July 27, 1997 and lasted 10 seasons with 214 episodes. Spin-offs include the animated television series <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate_Infinity" title="Stargate Infinity">Stargate Infinity</a></i>, the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate_Atlantis" title="Stargate Atlantis">Stargate Atlantis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate_Universe" title="Stargate Universe">Stargate Universe</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Direct-to-video" title="Direct-to-video">direct-to-DVD</a> films <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate:_The_Ark_of_Truth" title="Stargate: The Ark of Truth">Stargate: The Ark of Truth</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Stargate:_Continuum" title="Stargate: Continuum">Stargate: Continuum</a></i>. <i>Stargate SG-1</i> surpassed <i><a href="/wiki/The_X-Files" title="The X-Files">The X-Files</a></i> as the longest-running North American science fiction television series, a record later broken by <i><a href="/wiki/Smallville" title="Smallville">Smallville</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concern about the rapid pace of technological change crystallized around the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Technological_singularity" title="Technological singularity">technological singularity</a>, popularized by <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Marooned_in_Realtime" title="Marooned in Realtime">Marooned in Realtime</a></i> and then taken up by other authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_term_.22sci-fi.22">The term "sci-fi"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: The term "sci-fi"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Forrest_J_Ackerman" title="Forrest J Ackerman">Forrest J Ackerman</a> is credited with first using the term <i><b>sci-fi</b></i> (analogous to the then-trendy "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hi-fi" title="Hi-fi">hi-fi</a>") in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> As science fiction entered <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>, writers and fans active in the field came to associate the term with low-budget, low-tech "<a href="/wiki/B_movie" title="B movie">B-movies</a>" and with low-quality <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp science fiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> By the 1970s, critics within the field such as <a href="/wiki/Terry_Carr" title="Terry Carr">Terry Carr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damon_Knight" title="Damon Knight">Damon Knight</a> were using <i>sci-fi</i> to distinguish hack-work from serious science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_skiffy_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-wood_skiffy-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around 1978 critic <a href="/wiki/Susan_Wood_(science_fiction)" title="Susan Wood (science fiction)">Susan Wood</a> and others introduced the use of the odd pronunciation "<a href="/wiki/Skiffy" title="Skiffy">skiffy</a>" which is intended to be self-deprecating humor but is inconsistent with the documented genesis of the term "sci-fi" (i.e., one would not pronounce "hi-fi" as "hiffy") and Ackerman's own words engraved on his crypt plaque which read "Sci-Fi was My High".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Peter Nicholls writes that "SF" (or "sf") is "the preferred abbreviation within the community of sf writers and readers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicholls_sf_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicholls_sf-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Langford" title="David Langford">David Langford</a>'s monthly fanzine <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_End_of_Harry_Potter%3F" title="The End of Harry Potter?">Ansible</a></i> includes a regular section "As Others See Us" which offers numerous examples of "sci-fi" being used in a pejorative sense by people outside the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-As_Others..._43-0"><a href="#cite_note-As_Others...-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Innovation">Innovation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Innovation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Science fiction has criticized developing and future technologies, but also initiates <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a> and new technology. This topic has been more often discussed in literary and sociological than in scientific forums. Cinema and media theorist <a href="/wiki/Vivian_Sobchack" title="Vivian Sobchack">Vivian Sobchack</a> examines the dialogue between science fiction films and the technological imagination. Technology impacts artists and how they portray their fictionalized subjects, but the fictional world gives back to science by broadening imagination. <i><a href="/wiki/How_William_Shatner_Changed_the_World" title="How William Shatner Changed the World">How William Shatner Changed the World</a></i> is a documentary that gave a number of real-world examples of actualized technological imaginations. While more prevalent in the early years of science fiction with writers like <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, new authors still find ways to make currently impossible technologies seem closer to being realized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Fiction:_Bridge_between_the_Two_Cultures_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Fiction:_Bridge_between_the_Two_Cultures-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Categories">Categories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Categories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hard_science_fiction">Hard science fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hard science fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hard_science_fiction" title="Hard science fiction">Hard science fiction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="698" data-file-width="481" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg/200px-%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg/300px-%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg/400px-%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea%27_by_Neuville_and_Riou_024.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
'<a href="/wiki/Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea" title="Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea">Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea</a>' by Neuville and Riou 024, one of the earliest examples of hard science fiction.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hard science fiction, or "hard SF", is characterized by rigorous attention to accurate detail in the natural sciences, especially <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, or on accurately depicting worlds that more advanced technology may make possible. Some accurate predictions of the future come from the <a href="/wiki/Hard_science_fiction" title="Hard science fiction">hard science fiction</a> subgenre, but numerous inaccurate predictions have emerged as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some hard SF authors have distinguished themselves as working scientists, including <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Benford" title="Gregory Benford">Gregory Benford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_A._Landis" title="Geoffrey A. Landis">Geoffrey A. Landis</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Brin" title="David Brin">David Brin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Forward" title="Robert L. Forward">Robert L. Forward</a>, while mathematician authors include <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Rucker" title="Rudy Rucker">Rudy Rucker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a>. Other noteworthy hard SF authors include <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hal_Clement" title="Hal Clement">Hal Clement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greg_Bear" title="Greg Bear">Greg Bear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Niven" title="Larry Niven">Larry Niven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Sawyer" title="Robert J. Sawyer">Robert J. Sawyer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Baxter_(author)" title="Stephen Baxter (author)">Stephen Baxter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alastair_Reynolds" title="Alastair Reynolds">Alastair Reynolds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sheffield" title="Charles Sheffield">Charles Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bova" title="Ben Bova">Ben Bova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson">Kim Stanley Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anne_McCaffrey" title="Anne McCaffrey">Anne McCaffrey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andy_Weir" title="Andy Weir">Andy Weir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greg_Egan" title="Greg Egan">Greg Egan</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_science_fiction">Soft science fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Soft science fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soft_science_fiction" title="Soft science fiction">Soft science fiction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frankenstein,_pg_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1098" data-file-width="1510" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Frankenstein%2C_pg_7.jpg/260px-Frankenstein%2C_pg_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Frankenstein%2C_pg_7.jpg/390px-Frankenstein%2C_pg_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Frankenstein%2C_pg_7.jpg/520px-Frankenstein%2C_pg_7.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frankenstein,_pg_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mary Shelly's <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein: or, The Modern Prometheus</a></i>, one of the earliest examples of soft science fiction</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The description "soft" science fiction may describe works based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>. The term is sometimes used to describe improbable plots, absurd "science", and cardboard characters. Noteworthy writers in this category include <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sf_history_nvcc_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-sf_history_nvcc-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartwell_soft_def_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-hartwell_soft_def-46">[46]</a></sup> The term can describe stories focused primarily on character and emotion; SFWA Grand Master <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a> was an acknowledged master of this art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bradbury_master_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-bradbury_master-47">[47]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> produced a large quantity of social science fiction, including works by <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Polish</a> authors <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanislaw_Lem" title="Stanislaw Lem">Stanislaw Lem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Janusz_Zajdel" title="Janusz Zajdel">Janusz Zajdel</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> authors such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strugatsky_brothers" title="Strugatsky brothers">Strugatsky brothers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kir_Bulychov" title="Kir Bulychov">Kir Bulychov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yevgeny_Zamyatin" title="Yevgeny Zamyatin">Yevgeny Zamyatin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Yefremov" title="Ivan Yefremov">Ivan Yefremov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Some writers blur the boundary between hard and soft science fiction.</p>
<p>Related to social SF and soft SF are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopian" title="Utopian">utopian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dystopian" title="Dystopian">dystopian</a> stories; <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">Nineteen Eighty-Four</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">Brave New World</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Handmaid%27s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale">The Handmaid's Tale</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Oryx_and_Crake" title="Oryx and Crake">Oryx and Crake</a></i> are examples.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subgenres">Subgenres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Subgenres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(January 2013)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyberpunk">Cyberpunk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cyberpunk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk" title="Cyberpunk">Cyberpunk</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk_derivatives" title="Cyberpunk derivatives">Cyberpunk derivatives</a></div>
<p>The cyberpunk genre emerged in the early 1980s; combining <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punk_music" title="Punk music">punk</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Fact_and_Science_Fiction:_An_Encyclopedia_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Fact_and_Science_Fiction:_An_Encyclopedia-49">[49]</a></sup> the term was coined by author <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Bethke" title="Bruce Bethke">Bruce Bethke</a> for his 1980 <a href="/wiki/Short_story" title="Short story">short story</a> <i>Cyberpunk</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyber_def_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyber_def-50">[50]</a></sup> The time frame is usually near-future and the settings are often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dystopian" title="Dystopian">dystopian</a> in nature and characterized by misery. Common themes in cyberpunk include advances in <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">information technology</a> and especially the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, visually abstracted as <a href="/wiki/Cyberspace" title="Cyberspace">cyberspace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">cybernetics</a> and post-democratic societal control where corporations have more influence than governments. <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">Nihilism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-modernism" title="Post-modernism">post-modernism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">film noir</a> techniques are common elements, and the protagonists may be disaffected or reluctant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-hero" title="Anti-hero">anti-heroes</a>. Noteworthy authors in this genre are <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson" title="William Gibson">William Gibson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Sterling" title="Bruce Sterling">Bruce Sterling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson">Neal Stephenson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Cadigan" title="Pat Cadigan">Pat Cadigan</a>. James O'Ehley has called the 1982 film <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner" title="Blade Runner">Blade Runner</a></i> a definitive example of the <i>cyberpunk</i> visual style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blade_runner_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-blade_runner-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_travel">Time travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Time travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Time_travel_in_fiction" title="Time travel in fiction">Time travel in fiction</a></div>
<p>Time-travel stories have antecedents in the 18th and 19th centuries. The first major time-travel novel was <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Connecticut_Yankee_in_King_Arthur%27s_Court" title="A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court">A Connecticut Yankee in King Arthur's Court</a></i>. The most famous is <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>' 1895 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine">The Time Machine</a></i>, which uses a vehicle that allows an operator to travel purposefully and selectively, while Twain's time traveler is struck in the head. The term <i>time machine</i>, coined by Wells, is now universally used to refer to such a vehicle. <i><a href="/wiki/Back_to_the_Future" title="Back to the Future">Back to the Future</a></i> is one of the most popular movie franchises in this category; <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i> is a similarly popular long-running television franchise. Stories of this type are complicated by logical problems such as the <a href="/wiki/Grandfather_paradox" title="Grandfather paradox">grandfather paradox</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grandfather_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-grandfather-52">[52]</a></sup> as exemplified in the classic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Heinlein" title="Robert Heinlein">Robert Heinlein</a> story "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%80%94All_You_Zombies%E2%80%94" title="—All You Zombies—">—All You Zombies—</a>" and the <a href="/wiki/Futurama" title="Futurama">Futurama</a> episode "<a href="/wiki/Roswell_That_Ends_Well" title="Roswell That Ends Well">Roswell That Ends Well</a>." Time travel continues to be a popular subject in modern science fiction, in print, movies, and television.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternate_history">Alternate history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Alternate history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">Alternate history</a></div>
<p>Alternative history stories are based on the premise that historical events might have turned out differently. These stories may use time travel to change the past, or may simply set a story in a universe with a different history from our own. Classics in the genre include <i><a href="/wiki/Bring_the_Jubilee" title="Bring the Jubilee">Bring the Jubilee</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ward_Moore" title="Ward Moore">Ward Moore</a>, in which the South wins the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_the_High_Castle" title="The Man in the High Castle">The Man in the High Castle</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a>, in which Germany and Japan win <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sidewise_Award_for_Alternate_History" title="Sidewise Award for Alternate History">Sidewise Award</a> acknowledges the best works in this subgenre; the name is taken from <a href="/wiki/Murray_Leinster" title="Murray Leinster">Murray Leinster</a>'s 1934 story <i><a href="/wiki/Sidewise_in_Time" title="Sidewise in Time">Sidewise in Time</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Harry_Turtledove" title="Harry Turtledove">Harry Turtledove</a> is one of the most prominent authors in the subgenre and is sometimes called the "master of alternate history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_science_fiction">Military science fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Military science fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_science_fiction" title="Military science fiction">Military science fiction</a></div>
<p>Military science fiction is set in the context of conflict between national, interplanetary, or interstellar <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">armed forces</a>; the primary viewpoint characters are usually soldiers. Stories include detail about military technology, procedure, ritual, and history; military stories may use parallels with historical conflicts. Heinlein's <i><a href="/wiki/Starship_Troopers" title="Starship Troopers">Starship Troopers</a></i> is an early example, along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsai" title="Dorsai">Dorsai</a> novels of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_R._Dickson" title="Gordon R. Dickson">Gordon Dickson</a>. <a href="/wiki/Joe_Haldeman" title="Joe Haldeman">Joe Haldeman</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Forever_War" title="The Forever War">The Forever War</a></i> is a critique of the genre, a <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam</a>-era response to the World War II–style stories of earlier authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forever_war_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-forever_war-54">[54]</a></sup> Prominent military SF authors include <a href="/wiki/John_Scalzi" title="John Scalzi">John Scalzi</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Ringo" title="John Ringo">John Ringo</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Drake" title="David Drake">David Drake</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Weber" title="David Weber">David Weber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kratman" title="Tom Kratman">Tom Kratman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Z._Williamson" title="Michael Z. Williamson">Michael Z. Williamson</a>, <a href="/wiki/S._M._Stirling" title="S. M. Stirling">S. M. Stirling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_F._Carr" title="John F. Carr">John Carr</a>. The publishing company <a href="/wiki/Baen_Books" title="Baen Books">Baen Books</a> is known for cultivating several of these military science fiction authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baen_mil_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-baen_mil-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superhuman">Superhuman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Superhuman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Superhuman stories deal with the emergence of humans who have abilities beyond the normal. This can stem either from natural causes such as in <a href="/wiki/Olaf_Stapledon" title="Olaf Stapledon">Olaf Stapledon</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Odd_John" title="Odd John">Odd John</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Sturgeon" title="Theodore Sturgeon">Theodore Sturgeon</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/More_Than_Human" title="More Than Human">More Than Human</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Wylie" title="Philip Wylie">Philip Wylie</a>'s <i>Gladiator</i>, or be the result of scientific advances, such as the intentional augmentation in <a href="/wiki/A._E._van_Vogt" title="A. E. van Vogt">A. E. van Vogt</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Slan" title="Slan">Slan</a></i>. These stories usually focus on the alienation that these beings feel as well as society's reaction to them. These stories have played a role in the real life discussion of <a href="/wiki/Human_enhancement" title="Human enhancement">human enhancement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frederik_Pohl" title="Frederik Pohl">Frederik Pohl</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Man_Plus" title="Man Plus">Man Plus</a></i> also belongs to this category.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apocalyptic_and_post-apocalyptic">Apocalyptic and post-apocalyptic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Apocalyptic and post-apocalyptic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_and_post-apocalyptic_fiction" title="Apocalyptic and post-apocalyptic fiction">Apocalyptic and post-apocalyptic fiction</a></div>
<p>Apocalyptic fiction is concerned with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End_of_civilization" title="End of civilization">end of civilization</a> through war (<i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Beach_(novel)" title="On the Beach (novel)">On the Beach</a></i>), pandemic (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Man" title="The Last Man">The Last Man</a></i>), astronomic impact (<i><a href="/wiki/When_Worlds_Collide" title="When Worlds Collide">When Worlds Collide</a></i>), ecological disaster (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Wind_from_Nowhere" title="The Wind from Nowhere">The Wind from Nowhere</a></i>), or some other <a href="/wiki/Disaster" title="Disaster">general disaster</a> or with a world or civilization after such a disaster. Typical of the genre are <a href="/wiki/George_R._Stewart" title="George R. Stewart">George R. Stewart</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Earth_Abides" title="Earth Abides">Earth Abides</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Frank" title="Pat Frank">Pat Frank</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Alas,_Babylon" title="Alas, Babylon">Alas, Babylon</a></i>. Apocalyptic fiction generally concerns the disaster itself and the direct aftermath, while post-apocalyptic fiction can deal with anything from the near aftermath (as in <a href="/wiki/Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy">Cormac McCarthy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road" title="The Road">The Road</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Oryx_and_Crake" title="Oryx and Crake">Oryx and Crake</a></i> to centuries in the future (as in <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Vincent_Ben%C3%A9t" title="Stephen Vincent Benét">Stephen Vincent Benét</a>'s <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=By_The_Waters_of_Babylon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="By The Waters of Babylon (page does not exist)">By The Waters of Babylon</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavia_Butler" title="Octavia Butler">Octavia Butler</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Lilith%27s_Brood" title="Lilith's Brood">Lilith's Brood</a></i>) to hundreds or thousands of years in the future, as in <a href="/wiki/Russell_Hoban" title="Russell Hoban">Russell Hoban</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Riddley_Walker" title="Riddley Walker">Riddley Walker</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_M._Miller,_Jr." title="Walter M. Miller, Jr.">Walter M. Miller, Jr.</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Canticle_for_Leibowitz" title="A Canticle for Leibowitz">A Canticle for Leibowitz</a></i>. Apocalyptic science-fiction is a popular genre in video games. The critically acclaimed role-playing action adventure video game series <i><a href="/wiki/Fallout_(series)" title="Fallout (series)">Fallout</a></i> is set on a post-apocalyptic Earth, where civilization is recovering from a nuclear war as survivors struggle to survive and seek to rebuild society.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_opera">Space opera</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Space opera">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_opera" title="Space opera">Space opera</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Space_opera" title="Space opera">Space opera</a> is adventure science fiction set mainly or entirely in outer space or on sometimes distant planets. The conflict is heroic, and typically on a large scale. It is also used nostalgically, and modern space opera may be an attempt to recapture the <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_wonder" title="Sense of wonder">sense of wonder</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_science_fiction" title="Golden age of science fiction">golden age of science fiction</a>. The pioneer of this subgenre is generally recognized<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (January 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to be <a href="/wiki/E._E._Smith" title="E. E. Smith">Edward E. (Doc) Smith</a>, with his <i><a href="/wiki/Skylark_(series)" title="Skylark (series)">Skylark</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lensman" title="Lensman">Lensman</a></i> series. <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i> series is among the most popular and famous franchises in cinematic space opera. It covers epic battles between good and evil throughout an entire galaxy. <a href="/wiki/Alastair_Reynolds" title="Alastair Reynolds">Alastair Reynolds</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Revelation_Space" title="Revelation Space">Revelation Space</a></i> series, <a href="/wiki/Peter_F._Hamilton" title="Peter F. Hamilton">Peter F. Hamilton</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Void_Trilogy" title="Void Trilogy">Void</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Night%27s_Dawn" title="The Night's Dawn">Night's Dawn</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandora%27s_Star" title="Pandora's Star">Pandora's Star</a></i> series, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hunt_(author)" title="Stephen Hunt (author)">Stephen Hunt's</a> <i>Sliding Void</i> series, <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Fire_Upon_the_Deep" title="A Fire Upon the Deep">A Fire Upon the Deep</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Deepness_in_the_Sky" title="A Deepness in the Sky">A Deepness in the Sky</a></i> are newer examples of this genre.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Western">Space Western</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Space Western">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Western" title="Space Western">Space Western</a></div>
<p>The space Western transposes themes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Old_West" title="American Old West">American Western</a> books and films to a backdrop of futuristic space frontiers. These stories typically involve colony worlds that have only recently been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terraformed" title="Terraformed">terraformed</a> and/or settled serving as stand-ins for the backdrop of lawlessness and economic expansion that were predominant in the American west. Examples include the Sean Connery film <i><a href="/wiki/Outland_(film)" title="Outland (film)">Outland</a></i>, Heinlein's <i><a href="/wiki/Farmer_in_the_Sky" title="Farmer in the Sky">Farmer in the Sky</a></i>, Sparks Nevada: Marshall on Mars from the <a href="/wiki/Thrilling_Adventure_Hour" title="Thrilling Adventure Hour">Thrilling Adventure Hour</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Firefly_(TV_series)" title="Firefly (TV series)">Firefly</a></i> television series, and the film sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Serenity_(film)" title="Serenity (film)">Serenity</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Joss_Whedon" title="Joss Whedon">Joss Whedon</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Cowboy_Bebop" title="Cowboy Bebop">Cowboy Bebop</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Outlaw_Star" title="Outlaw Star">Outlaw Star</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Trigun" title="Trigun">Trigun</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_science_fiction">Social science fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Social science fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_science_fiction" title="Social science fiction">Social science fiction</a></div>
<p>Social science fiction focuses on themes of society and human nature in a science fiction setting. Since it usually focuses more on speculation about humanity and less on scientific accuracy, it's usually placed within <a href="/wiki/Soft_science_fiction" title="Soft science fiction">soft science fiction</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_fiction">Climate fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Climate fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_fiction" title="Climate fiction">Climate fiction</a></div>
<p>Climate fiction is a genre based around themes of reaction to major climate change. It is sometimes called "cli-fi", much as "science fiction" is often shortened to "sci-fi". Cli-fi novels and films are often set in either the present or the near or distant future, but they can also be set in the past. Many cli-fi works raise awareness about the major threats that <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> present to life on Earth.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mundane_science_fiction">Mundane science fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mundane science fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mundane_science_fiction" title="Mundane science fiction">Mundane science fiction</a></div>
<p>Mundane science fiction is a subgenre that is set on Earth and does not include outer space adventures or alien life. Because it focuses on modern day aspects, it is typically associated with <a href="/wiki/Hard_science_fiction" title="Hard science fiction">hard science fiction</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biopunk">Biopunk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Biopunk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biopunk" title="Biopunk">Biopunk</a></div>
<p><i>Biopunk</i> focuses on <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subversives" title="Subversives">subversives</a>. The main underlying theme within these stories is the attempt to change the human body and engineer humans for specific purposes through enhancements in genetic and molecular makeups. Many examples of this subgenre include subjects such as human experimentation, the misuse of biotechnology and synthetic biotechnology. This subgenre also includes works involving human cloning and how clones might exist within human society in the future.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_subgenres">Other subgenres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other subgenres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Anthropological_science_fiction" title="Anthropological science fiction">Anthropological science fiction</a></b> is a subgenre that absorbs and discusses anthropology and the study of human kind. Examples include <i>Hominids</i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Sawyer" title="Robert J. Sawyer">Robert J. Sawyer</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Neanderthal_(novel)" title="Neanderthal (novel)">Neanderthal</a></i> by John Darnton.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Kaiju" title="Kaiju">Kaiju</a></b> is a Japanese word that literally translates to "strange beast." The word has been translated and defined in English as "monster" and is used to refer to a genre of <i><a href="/wiki/Tokusatsu" title="Tokusatsu">tokusatsu</a></i> entertainment. <b>Kaiju</b> films feature large creatures of any form, usually attacking a major city or engaging another (or multiple) monster(s) in battle. The subgenre began in 1954 with <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Libertarian_science_fiction" title="Libertarian science fiction">Libertarian science fiction</a></b> is a subgenre focuses on the politics and social order implied by libertarian philosophies with an emphasis on individualism.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Comic_science_fiction" title="Comic science fiction">Comic science fiction</a></b> is a subgenre that exploits the genre's conventions for comic effect.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Feminist_science_fiction" title="Feminist science fiction">Feminist science fiction</a></b> poses questions about social issues such as how society constructs gender roles, the role reproduction plays in defining gender and the unequal political and personal power of men over women. Some of the most notable feminist science fiction works have illustrated these themes using <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopias</a> to explore a society in which gender differences or gender power imbalances do not exist, or <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopias</a> to explore worlds in which gender inequalities are intensified, thus asserting a need for feminist work to continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia3_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia3-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joanna_Russ" title="Joanna Russ">Joanna Russ</a>'s work, and some of <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a>'s work can be thus categorized. <a href="/wiki/Magical_feminism" title="Magical feminism">Magical feminism</a> is a subgenre of feminist science fiction.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Steampunk" title="Steampunk">Steampunk</a></b> is based on the idea of futuristic technology existing in the past, usually the 19th century, and often set in <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> England—but with prominent elements of either science fiction or <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a>, such as fictional technological inventions like those found in the works of <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>, or real technological developments like the computer occurring at an earlier date. Popular examples include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Difference_Engine" title="The Difference Engine">The Difference Engine</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson" title="William Gibson">William Gibson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Sterling" title="Bruce Sterling">Bruce Sterling</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(Westerfeld_novel)" title="Leviathan (Westerfeld novel)">Leviathan</a></i> series by <a href="/wiki/Scott_Westerfeld" title="Scott Westerfeld">Scott Westerfeld</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bas-Lag" title="Bas-Lag">Bas-Lag</a></i> series by <a href="/wiki/China_Mi%C3%A9ville" title="China Miéville">China Miéville</a>, as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Girl_Genius" title="Girl Genius">Girl Genius</a></i> web comic by <a href="/wiki/Phil_Foglio" title="Phil Foglio">Phil and Kaja Foglio</a>, although seeds of the subgenre may be seen in certain works of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moorcock" title="Michael Moorcock">Michael Moorcock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Jos%C3%A9_Farmer" title="Philip José Farmer">Philip José Farmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Stiles" title="Steve Stiles">Steve Stiles</a>, and in such games as <i><a href="/wiki/Space:_1889" title="Space: 1889">Space: 1889</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Rowland_(author)" title="Marcus Rowland (author)">Marcus Rowland's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Forgotten_Futures" title="Forgotten Futures">Forgotten Futures</a></i>. Machines are most often powered by steam in this genre (hence the name). <a href="/wiki/Terry_Gilliam" title="Terry Gilliam">Terry Gilliam</a>'s 1985 film <i><a href="/wiki/Brazil_(1985_film)" title="Brazil (1985 film)">Brazil</a></i> is seen as inspiration for writers and artists of the steampunk sub-culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_opera" title="Science fiction opera">Science fiction opera</a></b> is an opera in a science fiction setting without an outer space or multi-planetary setting, thereby distinguishing it from Space opera.</li>
<li><b>Sci-fi action</b> - Sharing many of the conventions of a <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">science fiction film</a>, sci-fi action films emphasizes gun-play, space battles, invented weaponry, and other sci-fi elements weaved into action film premises. Examples include <i><a href="/wiki/G.I._Samurai" title="G.I. Samurai">G.I. Samurai</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Terminator_2:_Judgment_Day" title="Terminator 2: Judgment Day">Terminator 2: Judgment Day</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix" title="The Matrix">The Matrix</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Total_Recall_(1990_film)" title="Total Recall (1990 film)">Total Recall</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Minority_Report_(film)" title="Minority Report (film)">Minority Report</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Island_(1980_film)" title="The Island (1980 film)">The Island</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Aliens_(film)" title="Aliens (film)">Aliens</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/I,_Robot_(film)" title="I, Robot (film)">I Robot</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transformers_(2007_film)" title="Transformers (2007 film)">Transformers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hunger_Games_(film)" title="The Hunger Games (film)">The Hunger Games</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Maze_Runner_(film)" title="The Maze Runner (film)">The Maze Runner</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_(film)" title="Equilibrium (film)">Equilibrium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/District_9" title="District 9">District 9</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Serenity_(film)" title="Serenity (film)">Serenity</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Akira_(1988_film)" title="Akira (1988 film)">Akira</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Paycheck_(film)" title="Paycheck (film)">Paycheck</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Predator_(film)" title="Predator (film)">Predator</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robocop" title="Robocop">Robocop</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Avatar_(2009_film)" title="Avatar (2009 film)">Avatar</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_Max_2" title="Mad Max 2">Mad Max 2</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Divergent_(film)" title="Divergent (film)">Divergent</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/They_Live" title="They Live">They Live</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Escape_From_New_York" title="Escape From New York">Escape From New York</a></i>,<i><a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Element" title="The Fifth Element">The Fifth Element</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Super_8" title="Super 8">Super 8</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></i></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/List_of_science_fiction_horror_films" title="List of science fiction horror films">Science fiction horror</a></b> – Often revolves around subjects that include but are not limited to killer aliens, mad scientists, and/or experiments gone wrong.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Dieselpunk" title="Dieselpunk">Dieselpunk</a></b> takes over where Steampunk leaves off. These are stories that take over as we usher in the machine-heavy eras of WWI and WWII. The use of diesel-powered machines plays heavily. In this (like its steam counterpart), the focus is on the technology.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Speculative_poetry" title="Speculative poetry">Science-fiction poetry</a></b> is poetry that has the characteristics or subject matter of science fiction. Science fiction poetry's main sources are the sciences and the literary movement of science fiction prose. An extended discussion of the field is given in <a href="/wiki/Suzette_Haden_Elgin" title="Suzette Haden Elgin">Suzette Haden Elgin</a>'s <i>The Science Fiction Poetry Handbook</i>, where she compares and contrasts it to both mainstream poetry and to prose science fiction. The former, she maintains, uses figures of speech unencumbered by noncompliant details, whereas these details can be key elements in science-fiction poetry.
