Prose in science fiction has the time to develop a setting and a story, whereas a poem in the field is normally constrained by its short length to rely on some device to get a point across quickly. Elgin says that the effectiveness of this kind of poetry pivots around the correct use of presupposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The Science Fiction Association is an international organization of speculative poets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> which gives the annual <a href="/wiki/Rhysling_Award" title="Rhysling Award">Rhysling Awards</a> for speculative poetry. An early example of science fiction in poetry is in <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Alfred, Lord Tennyson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Locksley_Hall" title="Locksley Hall">Locksley Hall</a></i>, where he introduces a picture of the future with "When I dipt into the future far as human eye could see...." This poem was written in 1835, near the end of the first <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Poetry was only sparingly published in traditional science-fiction outlets such as pulp magazines until the New Wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stableford.2C_Brian_p._267_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stableford.2C_Brian_p._267-61">[61]</a></sup> By the 1980s there were magazines specifically devoted to science-fiction poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stableford.2C_Brian_p._267_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stableford.2C_Brian_p._267-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_genres">Related genres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Related genres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_speculative_fiction.2C_fantasy.2C_and_horror">Other speculative fiction, fantasy, and horror</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Other speculative fiction, fantasy, and horror">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Speculative_fiction" title="Speculative fiction">Speculative fiction</a>.</div>
<p>The broader category of <a href="/wiki/Speculative_fiction" title="Speculative fiction">speculative fiction</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spec_fic_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-spec_fic-62">[62]</a></sup> includes science fiction, fantasy, <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">alternate histories</a> (which may have no particular scientific or futuristic component), and even literary stories that contain fantastic elements, such as the work of <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a> or <a href="/wiki/John_Barth" title="John Barth">John Barth</a>. For some editors, <a href="/wiki/Magic_realism" title="Magic realism">magic realism</a> is considered to be within the broad definition of speculative fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magic_realism_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-magic_realism-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fantasy">Fantasy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fantasy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">Fantasy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">Fantasy</a> is commonly associated with science fiction, and a number of writers have worked in both genres, while writers such as <a href="/wiki/Anne_McCaffrey" title="Anne McCaffrey">Anne McCaffrey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marion_Zimmer_Bradley" title="Marion Zimmer Bradley">Marion Zimmer Bradley</a> have written works that appear to blur the boundary between the two related genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccaffrey_sf_fantasy_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-mccaffrey_sf_fantasy-64">[64]</a></sup> The authors' professional organization is called the <a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy_Writers_of_America" title="Science Fiction and Fantasy Writers of America">Science Fiction and Fantasy Writers of America</a> (SFWA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFWA_info_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFWA_info-65">[65]</a></sup> SF conventions routinely have programming on fantasy topics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_author" title="Fantasy author">fantasy authors</a> such as <a href="/wiki/J._K._Rowling" title="J. K. Rowling">J. K. Rowling</a> have won the highest honor within the science fiction field, the <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Award</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fantasy_hugos_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-fantasy_hugos-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, science fiction differs from fantasy in that the former concerns things that might someday be possible or that at least embody the pretense of realism. Supernaturalism, usually absent in science fiction, is the distinctive characteristic of fantasy literature. A dictionary definition referring to fantasy literature is "fiction characterized by highly fanciful or supernatural elements." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dictionary_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-dictionary-68">[68]</a></sup> Examples of fantasy supernaturalism include magic (spells, harm to opponents), magical places (Narnia, Oz, Middle Earth, Hogwarts), supernatural creatures (witches, vampires, orcs, trolls), supernatural transportation (flying broomsticks, ruby slippers, windows between worlds), and shapeshifting (beast into man, man into wolf or bear, lion into sheep). Such things are basic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fantasy_tropes_and_conventions" title="Fantasy tropes and conventions">themes in fantasy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mythology_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-mythology-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Literary critic <a href="/wiki/Fredric_Jameson" title="Fredric Jameson">Fredric Jameson</a> has characterized the difference between the two genres by describing science fiction as turning "on a formal framework determined by concepts of the <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a> rather than those of religion" – that is, science fiction texts are bound by an inner logic based more on <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a> than on magic or the forces of good and evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archaeologies_of_the_Future:_This_Desire_Called_Utopia_and_Other_Science_Fictions_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archaeologies_of_the_Future:_This_Desire_Called_Utopia_and_Other_Science_Fictions-70">[70]</a></sup> Some narratives are described as being essentially science fiction but "with fantasy elements." The term "<a href="/wiki/Science_fantasy" title="Science fantasy">science fantasy</a>" is sometimes used to describe such material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci_fant_def_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci_fant_def-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_fantasy">Science fantasy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Science fantasy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_fantasy" title="Science fantasy">Science fantasy</a></div>
<p><i>Science fantasy</i> is a genre where elements of science fiction and fantasy co-exist or combine. Stories and franchises that display fictional science as well as supernatural elements, sorcery or/and "magical technology" are considered science fantasy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horror_fiction">Horror fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Horror fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">Horror fiction</a></div>
<p>Horror fiction is the literature of the unnatural and <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, with the aim of unsettling or frightening the reader, sometimes with <a href="/wiki/Graphic_violence" title="Graphic violence">graphic violence</a>. Historically it has also been known as <a href="/wiki/Weird_fiction" title="Weird fiction">weird fiction</a>. Although horror is not <i>per se</i> a branch of science fiction, some works of horror literature incorporates science fictional elements. One of the defining classical works of horror, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i>, is the first fully realized work of science fiction, where the manufacture of the monster is given a rigorous science-fictional grounding. The works of <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a> also helped define both the science fiction and the horror genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horror_orig_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-horror_orig-72">[72]</a></sup> Today horror is one of the most popular categories of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horror_films" title="Horror films">films</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horror_pop_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-horror_pop-73">[73]</a></sup> Horror is often mistakenly categorized as science fiction at the point of distribution by libraries, video rental outlets, etc.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernatural_fiction">Supernatural fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Supernatural fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supernatural_fiction" title="Supernatural fiction">Supernatural fiction</a></div>
<p>Supernatural fiction is a genre that features <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Paranormal" title="Paranormal">paranormal</a> phenomenon in stories and settings.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spy-Fi">Spy-Fi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Spy-Fi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spy-Fi_(subgenre)" title="Spy-Fi (subgenre)">Spy-Fi (subgenre)</a></div>
<p>A mixed genre that combines elements of science fiction with <a href="/wiki/Spy_fiction" title="Spy fiction">spy fiction</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mystery_fiction">Mystery fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Mystery fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mystery_fiction" title="Mystery fiction">Mystery fiction</a></div>
<p>Works in which science and technology are a dominant theme, but based on current reality, may be considered mainstream fiction. Much of the <a href="/wiki/Thriller_(genre)" title="Thriller (genre)">thriller genre</a> would be included, such as the novels of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Clancy" title="Tom Clancy">Tom Clancy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Michael_Crichton" title="Michael Crichton">Michael Crichton</a>, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Bond_(film_series)" title="James Bond (film series)">James Bond</a> films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mainstream_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-mainstream-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernist</a> works from writers like <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Vonnegut" title="Kurt Vonnegut">Kurt Vonnegut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Lem" title="Stanisław Lem">Stanisław Lem</a> have focused on speculative or <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existential</a> perspectives on contemporary reality and are on the borderline between SF and the mainstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modernism_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-modernism-75">[75]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Sawyer" title="Robert J. Sawyer">Robert J. Sawyer</a>, "Science fiction and mystery have a great deal in common. Both prize the intellectual process of puzzle solving, and both require stories to be plausible and hinge on the way things really do work."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mystery_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-mystery-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Mosley" title="Walter Mosley">Walter Mosley</a>, and other writers incorporate mystery elements in their science fiction, and vice versa.</p>
<p>Distinct from the above, a full-fledged Science Fiction Mystery is one which is set in a completely different world from ours, in which the circumstances and motives of the crime committed and the identity of the detective(s) seeking to solve it are of an essentially science fictional character. A prime example is <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/The_Caves_of_Steel" title="The Caves of Steel">The Caves of Steel</a>" and its sequels, set in a world thousands of years in the future and presenting the <a href="/wiki/Robot" title="Robot">Robot</a> detective <a href="/wiki/R._Daneel_Olivaw" title="R. Daneel Olivaw">R. Daneel Olivaw</a>. An allied genre is the Fantasy Mystery, a detective mystery set in a world of fantasy - such as the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Darcy" title="Lord Darcy">Lord Darcy</a> mysteries taking place in a world where magic works, or "<a href="/wiki/The_Idylls_of_the_Queen" title="The Idylls of the Queen">The Idylls of the Queen</a>" set in the mythical <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>'s court.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superhero_fiction">Superhero fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Superhero fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Superhero_fiction" title="Superhero fiction">Superhero fiction</a></div>
<p>Superhero fiction is a genre characterized by beings with much higher than usual capability and prowess, generally with a desire or need to help the citizens of their chosen country or world by using their powers to defeat natural or superpowered threats. A number of superhero fiction characters involve themselves (either intentionally or accidentally) with science fiction and fact, including advanced technologies, alien worlds, time travel, and interdimensional travel; but the standards of scientific plausibility are lower than with actual science fiction. Authors of this genre include <a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee" title="Stan Lee">Stan Lee</a> (co-creator of <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">Fantastic Four</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Man" title="Iron Man">Iron Man</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/X-Men" title="X-Men">X-Men</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Hulk_(comics)" title="Hulk (comics)">Hulk</a></i>); <a href="/wiki/Marv_Wolfman" title="Marv Wolfman">Marv Wolfman</a>, the creator of <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_(comics)" title="Blade (comics)">Blade</a></i> for Marvel Comics, and <i><a href="/wiki/Teen_Titans" title="Teen Titans">The New Teen Titans</a></i> for DC Comics; <a href="/wiki/Dean_Wesley_Smith" title="Dean Wesley Smith">Dean Wesley Smith</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Smallville" title="Smallville">Smallville</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/X-Men" title="X-Men">X-Men</a></i> novels) and <i><a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a></i> writers <a href="/wiki/Roger_Stern" title="Roger Stern">Roger Stern</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliot_S!_Maggin" title="Elliot S! Maggin">Elliot S! Maggin</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fandom_and_community">Fandom and community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Fandom and community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_fandom" title="Science fiction fandom">Science fiction fandom</a>.</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_fandom" title="Science fiction fandom">Science fiction fandom</a> is the "community of the literature of ideas... the culture in which new ideas emerge and grow before being released into society at large."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fandom_def_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-fandom_def-77">[77]</a></sup> Members of this community, "<a href="/wiki/Fan_(person)" title="Fan (person)">fans</a>", are in contact with each other at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_conventions" title="Science fiction conventions">conventions</a> or clubs, through print or online <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_fanzines" title="Science fiction fanzines">fanzines</a>, or on the Internet using web sites, <a href="/wiki/Mailing_list" title="Mailing list">mailing lists</a>, and other resources.</p>
<p>SF fandom emerged from the letters column in <i><a href="/wiki/Amazing_Stories" title="Amazing Stories">Amazing Stories</a></i> magazine. Soon fans began writing letters to each other, and then grouping their comments together in informal publications that became known as fanzines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fanzine_history_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-fanzine_history-78">[78]</a></sup> Once they were in regular contact, fans wanted to meet each other, and they organized local clubs. In the 1930s, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_conventions" title="Science fiction conventions">science fiction conventions</a> gathered fans from a wider area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fancyclopedia_con_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-fancyclopedia_con-79">[79]</a></sup> Conventions, clubs, and fanzines were the dominant form of fan activity, or "fanac", for decades, until the Internet facilitated communication among a much larger population of interested people.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authors">Authors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Authors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Science fiction is being written worldwide by a diverse population of authors. According to 2013 statistics by the science fiction publisher <a href="/wiki/Tor_Books" title="Tor Books">Tor Books</a>, men outnumber women by 78% to 22% among submissions to the publisher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puppygate" title="Puppygate">A controversy about voting slates</a> in the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Awards</a> highlighted tensions in the science fiction community between a trend of increasingly diverse works and authors being honored by awards, and a backlash by groups of authors and fans who preferred what they considered more traditional science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_A.V._Club_6_April_2015_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_A.V._Club_6_April_2015-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_science_fiction_awards" title="List of science fiction awards">List of science fiction awards</a>.</div>
<p>Among the most respected awards for science fiction are the <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Award</a>, presented by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Science_Fiction_Society" title="World Science Fiction Society">World Science Fiction Society</a> at Worldcon; the <a href="/wiki/Nebula_Award" title="Nebula Award">Nebula Award</a>, presented by SFWA and voted on by the community of authors; and the <a href="/wiki/John_W._Campbell_Memorial_Award_for_Best_Science_Fiction_Novel" title="John W. Campbell Memorial Award for Best Science Fiction Novel">John W. Campbell Memorial Award for Best Science Fiction Novel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Sturgeon_Award" title="Theodore Sturgeon Award">Theodore Sturgeon Memorial Award</a> for short fiction. One notable award for science fiction films is the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Award" title="Saturn Award">Saturn Award</a>. It is presented annually by The Academy of Science Fiction, Fantasy, and Horror Films.</p>
<p>There are national awards, like Canada's <a href="/wiki/Prix_Aurora_Awards" title="Prix Aurora Awards">Prix Aurora Awards</a>, regional awards, like the <a href="/wiki/Endeavour_Award" title="Endeavour Award">Endeavour Award</a> presented at Orycon for works from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a>, special interest or subgenre awards like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chesley_Award" title="Chesley Award">Chesley Award</a> for art or the <a href="/wiki/World_Fantasy_Award" title="World Fantasy Award">World Fantasy Award</a> for fantasy. Magazines may organize reader polls, notably the <a href="/wiki/Locus_Award" title="Locus Award">Locus Award</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventions.2C_clubs.2C_and_organizations">Conventions, clubs, and organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Conventions, clubs, and organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_convention" title="Science fiction convention">Science fiction convention</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="700" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg/280px-Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg/420px-Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg/560px-Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sfcon-reading-ddb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pamela Dean reading at <a href="/wiki/Minicon" title="Minicon">Minicon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Conventions (in fandom, shortened as "cons"), are held in cities around the world, catering to a local, regional, national, or international membership. General-interest conventions cover all aspects of science fiction, while others focus on a particular interest like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_fandom" title="Media fandom">media fandom</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filking" title="Filking">filking</a>, etc. Most are organized by volunteers in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-profit_organization" title="Non-profit organization">non-profit groups</a>, though most media-oriented events are organized by commercial promoters. The convention's activities are called the "program", which may include panel discussions, readings, autograph sessions, costume masquerades, and other events. Activities that occur throughout the convention are not part of the program; these commonly include a dealer's room, art show, and hospitality lounge (or "con suites").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-con_activities_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-con_activities-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conventions may host award ceremonies; <a href="/wiki/Worldcon" title="Worldcon">Worldcons</a> present the <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award" title="Hugo Award">Hugo Awards</a> each year. SF societies, referred to as "clubs" except in formal contexts, form a year-round base of activities for science fiction fans. They may be associated with an ongoing science fiction convention, or have regular club meetings, or both. Most groups meet in libraries, schools and universities, community centers, pubs or restaurants, or the homes of individual members. Long-established groups like the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Science_Fiction_Association" title="New England Science Fiction Association">New England Science Fiction Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Science_Fantasy_Society" title="Los Angeles Science Fantasy Society">Los Angeles Science Fantasy Society</a> have clubhouses for meetings and storage of convention supplies and research materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fan_clubhouse_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-fan_clubhouse-83">[83]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy_Writers_of_America" title="Science Fiction and Fantasy Writers of America">Science Fiction and Fantasy Writers of America</a> (SFWA) was founded by <a href="/wiki/Damon_Knight" title="Damon Knight">Damon Knight</a> in 1965 as a non-profit organization to serve the community of professional science fiction authors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFWA_info_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-SFWA_info-65">[65]</a></sup> 24 years after his essay "Unite or Fie!" had led to the organization of the <a href="/wiki/National_Fantasy_Fan_Federation" title="National Fantasy Fan Federation">National Fantasy Fan Federation</a>. Fandom has helped incubate related groups, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_fandom" title="Media fandom">media fandom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-media_fan_history_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-media_fan_history-84">[84]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Creative_Anachronism" title="Society for Creative Anachronism">Society for Creative Anachronism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sca_fandom_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-sca_fandom-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gamer" title="Gamer">gaming</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamer_fen_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamer_fen-86">[86]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filkers" title="Filkers">filking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Furry_fandom" title="Furry fandom">furry fandom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furry_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-furry-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fanzines_and_online_fandom">Fanzines and online fandom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Fanzines and online fandom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_fanzine" title="Science fiction fanzine">Science fiction fanzine</a>.</div>
<p>The first science fiction fanzine, <i>The Comet</i>, was published in 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_fanzine_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-first_fanzine-88">[88]</a></sup> Fanzine printing methods have changed over the decades, from the <a href="/wiki/Hectograph" title="Hectograph">hectograph</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mimeograph" title="Mimeograph">mimeograph</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ditto_machine" title="Ditto machine">ditto machine</a>, to modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocopying" title="Photocopying">photocopying</a>. Distribution volumes rarely justify the cost of commercial printing. Modern fanzines are printed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_printer" title="Computer printer">computer printers</a> or at local copy shops, or they may only be sent as <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">email</a>. The best known fanzine (or "'<a href="/wiki/Zine" title="Zine">zine</a>") today is <i><a href="/wiki/Ansible" title="Ansible">Ansible</a></i>, edited by <a href="/wiki/David_Langford" title="David Langford">David Langford</a>, winner of numerous Hugo awards. Other fanzines to win awards in recent years include <i><a href="/wiki/File_770" title="File 770">File 770</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mimosa_(magazine)" title="Mimosa (magazine)">Mimosa</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Plokta" title="Plokta">Plokta</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fan_awards_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-fan_awards-89">[89]</a></sup> Artists working for fanzines have risen to prominence in the field, including Brad W. Foster, Teddy Harvia, and Joe Mayhew; the Hugos include a category for <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award_for_Best_Fan_Artist" title="Hugo Award for Best Fan Artist">Best Fan Artists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fan_awards_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-fan_awards-89">[89]</a></sup> The earliest organized fandom online was the <a class="external text" href="http://www.noreascon.org/users/sflovers/u1/web/" rel="nofollow">SF Lovers</a> community, originally a mailing list in the late 1970s with a text <a href="/wiki/File_archiver" title="File archiver">archive file</a> that was updated regularly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sf-lovers_hist_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-sf-lovers_hist-90">[90]</a></sup> In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Usenet" title="Usenet">Usenet</a> groups greatly expanded the circle of fans online. In the 1990s, the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World-Wide_Web" title="World-Wide Web">World-Wide Web</a> exploded the community of online fandom by orders of magnitude, with thousands and then literally millions of web sites devoted to science fiction and related genres for all media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fan_clubhouse_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-fan_clubhouse-83">[83]</a></sup> Most such sites are small, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ephemeral" title="wikt:ephemeral">ephemeral</a>, and/or very narrowly focused, though sites like <a href="/wiki/SF_Site" title="SF Site">SF Site</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SF_Crowsnest" title="SF Crowsnest">SFcrowsnest</a> offer a broad range of references and reviews about science fiction.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fan_fiction">Fan fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Fan fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Fan_fiction" title="Fan fiction">Fan fiction</a>.</div>
<p>Fan fiction, known to aficionados as "fanfic", is <a href="/wiki/Non-commercial" title="Non-commercial">non-commercial</a> fiction created by fans in the setting of an established book, <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video game</a>, or television series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fanfic_def_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-fanfic_def-91">[91]</a></sup> This modern meaning of the term should not be confused with the traditional (pre-1970s) meaning of "fan fiction" within the community of <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_fandom" title="Science fiction fandom">fandom</a>, where the term meant original or parody fiction written by fans and published in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_fanzines" title="Science fiction fanzines">fanzines</a>, often with members of fandom as characters therein. Examples of this would include the Goon Defective Agency stories, written starting in 1956 by Irish fan John Berry and published in his and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Thomson_(fanzines)" title="Arthur Thomson (fanzines)">Arthur Thomson</a>'s fanzine <i>Retribution</i>. In the last few years, sites have appeared such as <a href="/wiki/Orion%27s_Arm" title="Orion's Arm">Orion's Arm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galaxiki" title="Galaxiki">Galaxiki</a>, which encourage collaborative development of science fiction universes. In some cases, the copyright owners of the books, films, or television series have instructed their lawyers to issue "cease and desist" letters to fans.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_fiction_studies">Science fiction studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Science fiction studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_studies" title="Science fiction studies">Science fiction studies</a>.</div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:180px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: center;">
<div style="margin:0px 2em;">
<p>[Science fiction] is the one real international literary form we have today, and as such has branched out to visual media, interactive media and on to whatever new media the world will invent in the 21st century... crossover issues between the sciences and the humanities are crucial for the century to come.</p>
</div>
<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">“</div>
<div style="position: absolute; right: 0px; bottom: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">”</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: center;"><a href="/wiki/George_Edgar_Slusser" title="George Edgar Slusser">George Edgar Slusser</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The study of science fiction, or <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_studies" title="Science fiction studies">science fiction studies</a>, is the critical assessment, interpretation, and discussion of science fiction literature, film, new media, fandom, and fan fiction. Science fiction scholars take science fiction as an object of study in order to better understand it and its relationship to science, technology, politics, and culture-at-large. Science fiction studies has a long history dating back to the turn of the 20th century, but it was not until later that science fiction studies solidified as a discipline with the publication of the academic journals <i><a href="/wiki/Extrapolation_(journal)" title="Extrapolation (journal)">Extrapolation</a></i> (1959), <i><a class="extiw" href="https://wikimediafoundation.org/wiki/The_International_Review_of_Science_Fiction" title="foundation:The International Review of Science Fiction">Foundation: The International Review of Science Fiction</a></i> (1972), and <i><a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_Studies" title="Science Fiction Studies">Science Fiction Studies</a></i> (1973), and the establishment of the oldest organizations devoted to the study of science fiction, the <a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_Research_Association" title="Science Fiction Research Association">Science Fiction Research Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_Foundation" title="Science Fiction Foundation">Science Fiction Foundation</a>, in 1970. The field has grown considerably since the 1970s with the establishment of more journals, organizations, and conferences with ties to the science fiction scholarship community, and science fiction degree-granting programs such as those offered by the University of Liverpool and Kansas University.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> has conducted surveys of "Public Attitudes and Public Understanding" of "Science Fiction and Pseudoscience."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-93">[93]</a></sup> They write that "Interest in science fiction may affect the way people think about or relate to science....one study found a strong relationship between preference for science fiction novels and support for the space program...The same study also found that students who read science fiction are much more likely than other students to believe that contacting extraterrestrial civilizations is both possible and desirable (Bainbridge 1982).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bainbridge_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bainbridge-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_serious_literature">As serious literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: As serious literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mary Shelley wrote a number of science fiction novels including <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i>, and is treated as a major Romantic writer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-introduction_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-introduction-95">[95]</a></sup> A number of science fiction works have received critical acclaim including <i><a href="/wiki/Childhood%27s_End" title="Childhood's End">Childhood's End</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Do_Androids_Dream_of_Electric_Sheep%3F" title="Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?">Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?</a></i> (the inspiration for the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Blade_Runner" title="Blade Runner">Blade Runner</a></i>). A number of respected writers of mainstream literature have written science fiction, including <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">Brave New World</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">Nineteen Eighty-Four</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Burgess" title="Anthony Burgess">Anthony Burgess</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/A_Clockwork_Orange_(novel)" title="A Clockwork Orange (novel)">A Clockwork Orange</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Handmaid%27s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale">The Handmaid's Tale</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_literature" title="Nobel Prize in literature">Nobel Laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/Doris_Lessing" title="Doris Lessing">Doris Lessing</a> wrote a series of SF novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Canopus_in_Argos" title="Canopus in Argos">Canopus in Argos</a></i>, and nearly all of <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Vonnegut" title="Kurt Vonnegut">Kurt Vonnegut</a>'s works contain science fiction premises or themes.</p>
<p>The scholar <a href="/wiki/Tom_Shippey" title="Tom Shippey">Tom Shippey</a> asks a perennial question of science fiction: "What is its relationship to fantasy fiction, is its readership still dominated by male adolescents, is it a taste which will appeal to the mature but non-eccentric literary mind?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ship_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ship-96">[96]</a></sup> In her much reprinted essay "Science Fiction and Mrs Brown,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harpercollins_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-harpercollins-97">[97]</a></sup> the science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Ursula_K._Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin</a> has approached an answer by first citing the essay written by the English author <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> entitled <i>Mr Bennett and Mrs Brown</i> in which she states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I believe that all novels, ... deal with character, and that it is to express character – not to preach doctrines, sing songs, or celebrate the glories of the British Empire, that the form of the novel, so clumsy, verbose, and undramatic, so rich, elastic, and alive, has been evolved ... The great novelists have brought us to see whatever they wish us to see through some character. Otherwise they would not be novelists, but poets, historians, or pamphleteers.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Le Guin argues that these criteria may be successfully applied to works of science fiction and so answers in the affirmative her rhetorical question posed at the beginning of her essay: "Can a science fiction writer write a novel?"</p>
<p>Tom Shippey<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ship_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-ship-96">[96]</a></sup> in his essay does not dispute this answer but identifies and discusses the essential differences that exists between a science fiction novel and one written outside the field. To this end, he compares George Orwell's <i><a href="/wiki/Coming_Up_for_Air" title="Coming Up for Air">Coming Up for Air</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Frederik_Pohl" title="Frederik Pohl">Frederik Pohl</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C._M._Kornbluth" title="C. M. Kornbluth">C. M. Kornbluth</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Space_Merchants" title="The Space Merchants">The Space Merchants</a></i> and concludes that the basic building block and distinguishing feature of a science fiction novel is the presence of the <i>novum</i>, a term <a href="/wiki/Darko_Suvin" title="Darko Suvin">Darko Suvin</a> adapts from <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Bloch" title="Ernst Bloch">Ernst Bloch</a> and defines as "a discrete piece of information recognizable as not-true, but also as not-unlike-true, not-flatly- (and in the current state of knowledge) impossible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metamorphoses_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-metamorphoses-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In science fiction the style of writing is often relatively clear and straightforward compared to classical literature. <a href="/wiki/Orson_Scott_Card" title="Orson Scott Card">Orson Scott Card</a>, an author of both science fiction and non-SF fiction, has postulated that in science fiction the message and intellectual significance of the work is contained within the story itself and, therefore, there need not be stylistic gimmicks or literary games; but that some writers and critics confuse clarity of language with lack of artistic merit. In Card's words:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...a great many writers and critics have based their entire careers on the premise that anything that the general public can understand without mediation is worthless drivel. [...] If everybody came to agree that stories should be told this clearly, the professors of literature would be out of a job, and the writers of obscure, encoded fiction would be, not honored, but pitied for their impenetrability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-google-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Science fiction author and physicist <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Benford" title="Gregory Benford">Gregory Benford</a> has declared that: "SF is perhaps the defining genre of the twentieth century, although its conquering armies are still camped outside the Rome of the literary citadels."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-september_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-september-100">[100]</a></sup> This sense of exclusion was articulated by <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Lethem" title="Jonathan Lethem">Jonathan Lethem</a> in an essay published in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Village_Voice" title="Village Voice">Village Voice</a></i> entitled "Close Encounters: The Squandered Promise of Science Fiction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encounters_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-encounters-101">[101]</a></sup> Lethem suggests that the point in 1973 when <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pynchon" title="Thomas Pynchon">Thomas Pynchon</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Gravity%27s_Rainbow" title="Gravity's Rainbow">Gravity's Rainbow</a></i> was nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Nebula_Award" title="Nebula Award">Nebula Award</a>, and was passed over in favor of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Rendezvous_with_Rama" title="Rendezvous with Rama">Rendezvous with Rama</a></i>, stands as "a hidden tombstone marking the death of the hope that SF was about to merge with the mainstream." Among the responses to Lethem was one from the editor of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magazine_of_Fantasy_and_Science_Fiction" title="Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction">Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction</a></i> who asked: "When is it [the SF genre] ever going to realize it can't win the game of trying to impress the mainstream?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfsite_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfsite-102">[102]</a></sup> On this point the journalist and author <a href="/wiki/David_Barnett_(writer)" title="David Barnett (writer)">David Barnett</a> has remarked:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The ongoing, endless war between "literary" fiction and "genre" fiction has well-defined lines in the sand. Genre's foot soldiers think that literary fiction is a collection of meaningless but prettily drawn pictures of the human condition. The literary guard consider genre fiction to be crass, commercial, whizz-bang potboilers. Or so it goes.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Barnett, in an earlier essay had pointed to a new development in this "endless war":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian4_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian4-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What do novels about a journey across post-apocalyptic America, a clone waitress rebelling against a future society, a world-girdling pipe of special gas keeping mutant creatures at bay, a plan to rid a colonizable new world of dinosaurs, and genetic engineering in a collapsed civilization have in common?<br/></p>
<p>They are all most definitely not science fiction.<br/>
Literary readers will probably recognize <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road" title="The Road">The Road</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy">Cormac McCarthy</a>, one of the sections of <i><a href="/wiki/Cloud_Atlas_(novel)" title="Cloud Atlas (novel)">Cloud Atlas</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/David_Mitchell_(author)" title="David Mitchell (author)">David Mitchell</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gone-Away_World" title="The Gone-Away World">The Gone-Away World</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Nick_Harkaway" title="Nick Harkaway">Nick Harkaway</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stone_Gods_(novel)" title="The Stone Gods (novel)">The Stone Gods</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jeanette_Winterson" title="Jeanette Winterson">Jeanette Winterson</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Oryx_and_Crake" title="Oryx and Crake">Oryx and Crake</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood">Margaret Atwood</a> from their descriptions above. All of these novels use the tropes of what most people recognize as science fiction, but their authors or publishers have taken great pains to ensure that they are not categorized as such.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World-wide_examples">World-wide examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: World-wide examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although perhaps most developed as a genre and community in the United States, Canada, and the United Kingdom, science fiction is a worldwide phenomenon. Organisations devoted to promotion and even translation in particular countries are commonplace, as are country- or language-specific genre awards.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Dib" title="Mohammed Dib">Mohammed Dib</a>, an Algerian writer, wrote a science fiction <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a> about his nation's politics, <i>Qui se souvient de la mer</i> (<i>Who Remembers the Sea?</i>) in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mohammed_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-mohammed-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Masimba_Musodza" title="Masimba Musodza">Masimba Musodza</a>, a Zimbabwean author, published <i>MunaHacha Maive Nei?</i> the first science-fiction novel in the Shona language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> which also holds the distinction of being the first novel in the Shona language to appear as an ebook first before it came out in print. In <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, a movie titled <i><a href="/wiki/District_9" title="District 9">District 9</a></i> came out in 2009, an <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> allegory featuring <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> forms, produced by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jackson" title="Peter Jackson">Peter Jackson</a>.</p>
<p>Science fiction examines society through shifting power structures (such as the shift of power from humanity to alien overlords). African science fiction often uses this genre norm to situate slavery and the slave trade as an alien abduction. Commonalities in experiences with unknown languages, customs, and culture lend themselves well to this comparison. The subgenre also commonly employs the mechanism of time travel to examine the effects of slavery and forced emigration on the individual and the family.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bengali_science_fiction" title="Bengali science fiction">Bengali science fiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_science_fiction" title="Chinese science fiction">Chinese science fiction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_science_fiction" title="Japanese science fiction">Japanese science fiction</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Indian science fiction, defined loosely as science fiction by writers of Indian descent, began with the English-language publication of Kylas Chundar Dutt's <i>A Journal of Forty-Eight Hours of the Year 1945</i> in the <i>Calcutta Literary Gazette</i> (June 6, 1835). Since this story was intended as a political polemic, credit for the first science fiction story is often given to later Bengali authors such as <a href="/wiki/Jagadananda_Roy" title="Jagadananda Roy">Jagadananda Roy</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hemlal_Dutta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hemlal Dutta (page does not exist)">Hemlal Dutta</a> and the polymath <a href="/wiki/Jagadish_Chandra_Bose" title="Jagadish Chandra Bose">Jagadish Chandra Bose</a>. Eminent film maker and writer <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a> also enriched Bengali science fiction by writing many short stories as well as science fiction series, <a href="/wiki/Professor_Shonku" title="Professor Shonku">Professor Shonku</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Bengali_science_fiction" title="Bengali science fiction">Bengali science fiction</a>). Similar traditions exist in Hindi, Marathi, Tamil and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-literaturaprospectiva_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-literaturaprospectiva-107">[107]</a></sup> In English, the modern era of Indian speculative fiction began with the works of authors such as <a href="/wiki/Samit_Basu" title="Samit Basu">Samit Basu</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Payal_Dhar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Payal Dhar (page does not exist)">Payal Dhar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vandana_Singh" title="Vandana Singh">Vandana Singh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anil_Menon" title="Anil Menon">Anil Menon</a>. Works such as <a href="/wiki/Amitav_Ghosh" title="Amitav Ghosh">Amitav Ghosh</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Calcutta_Chromosome" title="The Calcutta Chromosome">The Calcutta Chromosome</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie">Salman Rushdie</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Grimus" title="Grimus">Grimus</a></i>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Boman_Desai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Boman Desai (page does not exist)">Boman Desai</a>'s <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Memory_of_Elephants&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Memory of Elephants (page does not exist)">The Memory of Elephants</a></i> are generally classified as magic realist works but make essential use of SF tropes and techniques. In recent years authors in some other Indian languages have begun telling stories in this genre; for example in Punjabi IP Singh and <a href="/wiki/Rupinderpal_Singh_Dhillon" title="Rupinderpal Singh Dhillon">Roop Dhillon</a> have written stories that can clearly be defined as Punjabi science fiction. The latter has coined the term Vachitarvaad to describe such literature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Bangladesh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_(16519100026).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1264" data-file-width="892" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_%2816519100026%29.jpg/170px-Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_%2816519100026%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_%2816519100026%29.jpg/255px-Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_%2816519100026%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_%2816519100026%29.jpg/340px-Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_%2816519100026%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Book_Fair_2015_Dhaka_(16519100026).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Muhammed_Zafar_Iqbal" title="Muhammed Zafar Iqbal">Muhammed Zafar Iqbal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ekushey_Book_Fair" title="Ekushey Book Fair">Ekushey Book Fair</a>, Dhaka in February 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> has a strong <a href="/wiki/Bengali_science_fiction" title="Bengali science fiction">Science fiction</a> literature in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>. After Qazi Abdul Halim's <i>Mohasunner Kanna</i> (<i>Tears of the Cosmos</i>) (1970), <a href="/wiki/Humayun_Ahmed" title="Humayun Ahmed">Humayun Ahmed</a> wrote the first modern <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a> SF novel, <i>Tomader Jonno Valobasa</i> (<i>Love For You All</i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It was published in 1973. This book is treated as the first full-fledged Bangladeshi science fiction novel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Then he wrote <i>Tara Tinjon</i> (<i>They were Three</i>), <i>Irina</i>, <i>Anonto Nakshatra Bithi</i> (<i>Endless Galaxy</i>), <i>Fiha Somikoron</i> (<i>Fiha Equation</i>) etc.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>But Bengali science fiction leaves its cocoon phase holding the hands of <a href="/wiki/Muhammed_Zafar_Iqbal" title="Muhammed Zafar Iqbal">Muhammed Zafar Iqbal</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Mr. Iqbal wrote a story named <i>Copotronic Sukh Dukho</i> (<i>Copotronic Emotions</i>) when he was a student of Dhaka University.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This story was later included in a compilation of Iqbal's work in a book by the same name. <a href="/wiki/Muktodhara" title="Muktodhara">Muktodhara</a>, a famous publishing house of <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> was the publisher of this book. This collection of science fiction stories gained huge popularity and the new trend of science fiction emerged among Bengali writers and readers. After his first collection, Mr. Iqbal transformed his own science fiction cartoon strip <i>Mohakashe Mohatrash</i> (<i>Terror in the Cosmos</i>) into a novel. All told, Muhammed Zafar Iqbal has written the greatest number of science fiction works in Bengali science fiction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Following the footsteps of the ancestors, more and more writers, especially young writers started writing science fiction and a new era started in Bengali literature.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Modern science fiction in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> mainly depends on the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_World" title="Science Fiction World">Science Fiction World</a></i>. A number of works were originally published in it in installments, including the highly successful novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three-Body_Problem" title="The Three-Body Problem">The Three-Body Problem</a></i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Liu_Cixin" title="Liu Cixin">Liu Cixin</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Until recently, there has been little domestic science fiction literature in Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Within the small field, the author and critic writing under the nom de plume <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Djuna&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Djuna (page does not exist)">Djuna</a> has been credited with being the major force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kim_Boyoung&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kim Boyoung (page does not exist)">Kim Boyoung</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bae_Myunghoon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bae Myunghoon (page does not exist)">Bae Myunghoon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kwak_Jaesik" title="Kwak Jaesik">Kwak Jaesik</a> are also often mentioned as the new generation of Korean science fiction writers of 2010s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The upswing that began in 2009 has been attributed by Shin Junebong to a combination of factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-list.or.kr_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-list.or.kr-111">[111]</a></sup> Shin quotes Djuna as saying, "'It looks like the various literary awards established by one newspaper after another, with hefty sums of prize money, had a big impact.'" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-list.or.kr_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-list.or.kr-111">[111]</a></sup> Another factor cited was the active use of Web bulletin boards among the then-young writers brought up on translations of Western SF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In spite of the increase, there were still no more than sixty or so authors writing in the field at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-list.or.kr_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-list.or.kr-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_east">Middle east</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Middle east">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chalomot_Be%27aspamia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chalomot Be'aspamia (page does not exist)">Chalomot Be'aspamia</a></i> is an <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israeli</a> magazine of short science fiction and fantasy stories. <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Prophecies_Of_Karma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Prophecies Of Karma (page does not exist)">The Prophecies Of Karma</a></i>, published in 2011, is advertised as the first work of science fiction by an <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> author, the <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanese</a> writer <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nael_Gharzeddine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nael Gharzeddine (page does not exist)">Nael Gharzeddine</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Croatian_science_fiction" title="Croatian science fiction">Croatian science fiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_science_fiction_and_fantasy" title="Czech science fiction and fantasy">Czech science fiction and fantasy</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_science_fiction" title="French science fiction">French science fiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_science_fiction" title="Norwegian science fiction">Norwegian science fiction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_in_Poland" title="Science fiction in Poland">Science fiction in Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_science_fiction" title="Romanian science fiction">Romanian science fiction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_in_Russia" title="Science fiction in Russia">Science fiction in Russia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_in_Serbia" title="Science fiction in Serbia">Science fiction in Serbia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_science_fiction" title="Spanish science fiction">Spanish science fiction</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France_and_Belgium">France and Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: France and Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="257" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg/220px-Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Moonshot from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune" title="Le Voyage dans la lune">Le Voyage dans la lune</a></i> (1902), a silent film by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="George Méliès">George Méliès</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>, a 19th-century French novelist known for his pioneering science fiction works (<i><a href="/wiki/Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea" title="Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea">Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon" title="From the Earth to the Moon">From the Earth to the Moon</a></i>) is the prime representative of the French legacy of science fiction. French science fiction of the 19th century was also represented with such artists as <a href="/wiki/Albert_Robida" title="Albert Robida">Albert Robida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ignace_Isidore_G%C3%A9rard_Grandville" title="Jean Ignace Isidore Gérard Grandville">Isidore Grandville</a>. In the 20th century, traditions of French science fiction were carried on by writers like <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Boulle" title="Pierre Boulle">Pierre Boulle</a> (best known for his <i><a href="/wiki/Planet_of_the_Apes_(novel)" title="Planet of the Apes (novel)">Planet of the Apes</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Serge_Brussolo" title="Serge Brussolo">Serge Brussolo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Werber" title="Bernard Werber">Bernard Werber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Barjavel" title="René Barjavel">René Barjavel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Merle" title="Robert Merle">Robert Merle</a>, among others.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bande_dessin%C3%A9e" title="Bande dessinée">bande dessinée</a></i> ("BD") science-fiction is a well established genre.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Notable French science fiction comics include <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valerian_et_Laureline" title="Valerian et Laureline">Valerian et Laureline</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Christin" title="Pierre Christin">Pierre Christin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_M%C3%A9zi%C3%A8res" title="Jean-Claude Mézières">Jean-Claude Mézières</a>, a space opera franchise that has lasted since 1967. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_Hurlant" title="Metal Hurlant">Metal Hurlant</a></i> magazine (known in US as <i><a href="/wiki/Heavy_Metal_(magazine)" title="Heavy Metal (magazine)">Heavy Metal</a></i>) was one of the largest contributors to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francophone" title="Francophone">francophone</a> science-fiction comics. Its major authors include <a href="/wiki/Jean_Giraud" title="Jean Giraud">Jean "Moebius" Giraud</a>, creator of <i><a href="/wiki/Arzach" title="Arzach">Arzach</a></i>; Chilean <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Jodorowsky" title="Alejandro Jodorowsky">Alejandro Jodorowsky</a>, who created a series of comics, including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%27Incal" title="L'Incal">L'Incal</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Metabarons" title="Metabarons">Les Metabarons</a></i>, set in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jodoverse" title="Jodoverse">Jodoverse</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Enki_Bilal" title="Enki Bilal">Enki Bilal</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nikopol_Trilogy" title="The Nikopol Trilogy">The Nikopol Trilogy</a></i>. Giraud also contributed to French SF animation, collaborating with <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Laloux" title="René Laloux">René Laloux</a> on several animated features. A number of artists from neighboring countries, such as Spain and Italy, create science fiction and fantasy comics in French aimed at a Franco-Belgian market.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_cinema" title="French cinema">French cinema</a>, science fiction began with silent film director and visual effects pioneer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="George Méliès">George Méliès</a>, whose most famous film was <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voyage_to_the_Moon" title="Voyage to the Moon">Voyage to the Moon</a></i>, loosely based on books by Verne and Wells. In the 20th and 21st centuries, French science fiction films were represented by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Laloux" title="René Laloux">René Laloux</a>'s animated features, as well as Enki Bilal's adaptation of the Nikopol Trilogy, <i><a href="/wiki/Immortal_(2004_film)" title="Immortal (2004 film)">Immortal</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Luc_Besson" title="Luc Besson">Luc Besson</a> filmed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Element" title="The Fifth Element">The Fifth Element</a></i> as a joint Franco-American production.</p>
<p>In the French-speaking world, the colloquial use of the term <i>sci-fi</i> is an accepted <a href="/wiki/Anglicism" title="Anglicism">Anglicism</a> for the term <i>science fiction</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> This probably stems from the fact that science fiction writing never expanded there to the extent it did in the English-speaking world, particularly with the dominance of the United States. Nevertheless, France has made a tremendous contribution to science fiction in its seminal stages of development. Although the term "science fiction" is understood in France, their penchant for the "weird and wacky" has a long tradition and is sometimes called "le culte du merveilleux." This uniquely French tradition certainly encompasses what the anglophone world would call <a href="/wiki/French_science_fiction" title="French science fiction">French science fiction</a> but also ranges across <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairies" title="Fairies">fairies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dadaism" title="Dadaism">Dadaism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surrealism</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> has a vivid history in science fiction, though almost unknown outside her borders. The history of Italian science fiction recognizes a varied roadmap of this genre which spread to a popular level after World War Two, and in particular in the second half of the 1950s, on the wave of American and British literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest pioneers may be found in the literature of the fantastic voyage and of the Renaissance Utopia, even in previous masterpieces such as "The Million" of <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>. In the second half of the 19th century stories and short novels of "scientific fantasies" (also known as "incredible stories" or "fantastic" or "adventuristic", "novels of the future times" or "utopic", "of the tomorrow") appeared in Sunday newspaper supplements, in literary magazines, and as booklets published in installments. Added to these, at the beginning of the 20th century, were the most futuristic masterpieces of the great <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Salgari" title="Emilio Salgari">Emilio Salgari</a>, considered by most the father of Italian science fiction, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Enrico_Novelli&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Enrico Novelli (page does not exist)">Yambo</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Luigi_Motta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Luigi Motta (page does not exist)">Luigi Motta</a>, well known authors of popular novels of the time, with extraordinary adventures in remote and exotic places, and even works of authors representing known figures of the "top" literature, among them <a href="/wiki/Massimo_Bontempelli" title="Massimo Bontempelli">Massimo Bontempelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Capuana" title="Luigi Capuana">Luigi Capuana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guido_Gozzano" title="Guido Gozzano">Guido Gozzano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ercole_Luigi_Morselli" title="Ercole Luigi Morselli">Ercole Luigi Morselli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The true birth of Italian science fiction is placed in 1952, with the publishing of the first specialized magazines, <i>Scienza Fantastica</i> (Fantastic Science) and <i>Urania,</i> and with the appearance of the term "<i>fantascienza</i>" which has become the usual translation of the English term "science fiction." The "Golden Years" span the period 1957-1960.</p>
<p>From the end of the 1950s science fiction became in Italy one of the most popular genres, although its popular success was not followed by critical success. In spite of an active and organized fandom there hasn't been an authentic sustained interest on the part of the Italian cultural élite towards science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Popular Italian science fiction writers include <a href="/wiki/Gianluigi_Zuddas" title="Gianluigi Zuddas">Gianluigi Zuddas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giampietro_Stocco" title="Giampietro Stocco">Giampietro Stocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lino_Aldani" title="Lino Aldani">Lino Aldani</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> as well as comic artists, such as <a href="/wiki/Milo_Manara" title="Milo Manara">Milo Manara</a>. <a href="/wiki/Valerio_Evangelisti" title="Valerio Evangelisti">Valerio Evangelisti</a> is the best known modern author of Italian science fiction and fantasy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucca_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucca-118">[118]</a></sup> Also, popular Italian children's writer <a href="/wiki/Gianni_Rodari" title="Gianni Rodari">Gianni Rodari</a> often turned to science fiction aimed at children, most notably, in <i>Gip in the Television</i>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="963" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg/220px-Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg/330px-Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg/440px-Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fritz_Lang_et_Curt_Courant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Director <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Lang" title="Fritz Lang">Fritz Lang</a> and cameraman <a href="/wiki/Curt_Courant" title="Curt Courant">Curt Courant</a>, creators of <i><a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(1927_film)" title="Metropolis (1927 film)">Metropolis</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
