<p>The main German science fiction writer in the 19th century was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurd_La%C3%9Fwitz" title="Kurd Laßwitz">Kurd Laßwitz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> According to Austrian SF critic <a href="/wiki/Franz_Rottensteiner" title="Franz Rottensteiner">Franz Rottensteiner</a>, though significant German novels of a science-fiction nature were published in the first half of the 20th century, SF did not exist as a genre in the country until after World War II and the heavy importing and translation of American works. In the 20th century, during the years of divided Germany, both East and West spawned a number of successful writers. Top East German writers included <a href="/wiki/Angela_Steinm%C3%BCller" title="Angela Steinmüller">Angela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karlheinz_Steinm%C3%BCller" title="Karlheinz Steinmüller">Karlheinz Steinmüller</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther_Krupkat" title="Günther Krupkat">Günther Krupkat</a>. West German authors included <a href="/wiki/Carl_Amery" title="Carl Amery">Carl Amery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gudrun_Pausewang" title="Gudrun Pausewang">Gudrun Pausewang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Jeschke" title="Wolfgang Jeschke">Wolfgang Jeschke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sch%C3%A4tzing" title="Frank Schätzing">Frank Schätzing</a>, among others. A well known science fiction book series in the German language is <i><a href="/wiki/Perry_Rhodan" title="Perry Rhodan">Perry Rhodan</a></i>, which started in 1961. Having sold over two billion copies (in <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp</a>, paperback and hardcover formats), it is the most successful science fiction book series ever written, worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry_Rhodan_35th_anniversary_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perry_Rhodan_35th_anniversary-120">[120]</a></sup> Current well-known SF authors from Germany are five-time <i><a href="/wiki/Kurd-La%C3%9Fwitz-Preis" title="Kurd-Laßwitz-Preis">Kurd-Laßwitz-Award</a></i> winner <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Eschbach" title="Andreas Eschbach">Andreas Eschbach</a>, whose books <i><a href="/wiki/The_Carpet_Makers" title="The Carpet Makers">The Carpet Makers</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Eine_Billion_Dollar" title="Eine Billion Dollar">Eine Billion Dollar</a></i> are big successes, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sch%C3%A4tzing" title="Frank Schätzing">Frank Schätzing</a>, who in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Swarm_(Sch%C3%A4tzing_novel)" title="The Swarm (Schätzing novel)">The Swarm</a></i> mixes elements of the science thriller with SF elements to an apocalyptic scenario. The most prominent German-speaking author, according to <i><a href="/wiki/Die_Zeit" title="Die Zeit">Die Zeit</a></i>, is<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2013)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbert_W._Franke" title="Herbert W. Franke">Herbert W. Franke</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In the 1920s Germany produced a number of critically acclaimed high-budget science fiction and horror films. <i><a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(1927_film)" title="Metropolis (1927 film)">Metropolis</a></i> by director <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Lang" title="Fritz Lang">Fritz Lang</a> is credited as one of the most influential science fiction films ever made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Other films of the era included <i><a href="/wiki/Woman_in_the_Moon" title="Woman in the Moon">Woman in the Moon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alraune_(1928_film)" title="Alraune (1928 film)">Alraune</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Algol_(film)" title="Algol (film)">Algol</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gold_(1934_film)" title="Gold (1934 film)">Gold</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_World_(1934_film)" title="Master of the World (1934 film)">Master of the World</a></i>, among others. In the second half of the 20th century, East Germany also became a major science fiction film producer, often in a collaboration with fellow Eastern Bloc countries. Films of this era include <i><a href="/wiki/Eolomea" title="Eolomea">Eolomea</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/First_Spaceship_on_Venus" title="First Spaceship on Venus">First Spaceship on Venus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hard_to_Be_a_God_(1989_film)" title="Hard to Be a God (1989 film)">Hard to Be a God</a></i>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia_and_ex-Soviet_countries">Russia and ex-Soviet countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Russia and ex-Soviet countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Russian_science_fiction_and_fantasy" title="Russian science fiction and fantasy">Russian science fiction and fantasy</a></div>
<p>Russians made their first steps to science fiction in the mid-19th century, with <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopias</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faddei_Bulgarin" title="Faddei Bulgarin">Faddei Bulgarin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vladimir_Odoevsky" title="Vladimir Odoevsky">Vladimir Odoevsky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> However, it was the Soviet era that became the genre's golden age. Soviet writers were prolific,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> despite limitations set up by state censorship. Early Soviet writers, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Belayev" title="Alexander Belayev">Alexander Belayev</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexey_N._Tolstoy" title="Alexey N. Tolstoy">Alexey N. Tolstoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Obruchev" title="Vladimir Obruchev">Vladimir Obruchev</a>, employed Vernian/Wellsian <a href="/wiki/Hard_science_fiction" title="Hard science fiction">hard science fiction</a> based on scientific predictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HD_SF_Lit_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-HD_SF_Lit-124">[124]</a></sup> The most notable books of the era include Belayev's <i><a href="/wiki/Amphibian_Man" title="Amphibian Man">Amphibian Man</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Air_Seller" title="The Air Seller">The Air Seller</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Professor_Dowell%27s_Head" title="Professor Dowell's Head">Professor Dowell's Head</a></i>; Tolstoy's <i><a href="/wiki/Aelita_(novel)" title="Aelita (novel)">Aelita</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engineer_Garin%27s_Death_Ray" title="Engineer Garin's Death Ray">Engineer Garin's Death Ray</a></i>. Early Soviet science fiction was influenced by <a href="/wiki/List_of_communist_ideologies" title="List of communist ideologies">communist ideology</a> and often featured a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">leftist</a> agenda or <a href="/wiki/Anti-capitalism" title="Anti-capitalism">anti-capitalist</a> satire. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Those few early Soviet books that challenged the communist worldview and satirized the Soviets, such as <a href="/wiki/Yevgeny_Zamyatin" title="Yevgeny Zamyatin">Yevgeny Zamyatin</a>'s dystopia <i><a href="/wiki/We_(novel)" title="We (novel)">We</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bulgakov" title="Mikhail Bulgakov">Mikhail Bulgakov</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_of_a_Dog" title="Heart of a Dog">Heart of a Dog</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatal_Eggs" title="Fatal Eggs">Fatal Eggs</a></i>, were banned from publishing until the 1980s, although they still circulated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fan-made" title="Fan-made">fan-made</a> copies.</p>
<p>In the second half of the 20th century, a new generation of writers developed a more complex approach. <a href="/wiki/Social_science_fiction" title="Social science fiction">Social science fiction</a>, concerned with philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopian</a> ideas, became the prevalent subgenre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The breakthrough was started by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Yefremov" title="Ivan Yefremov">Ivan Yefremov</a>'s utopian novel <i><a href="/wiki/Andromeda_(novel)" title="Andromeda (novel)">Andromeda Nebula</a></i> (1957). He was soon followed by brothers <a href="/wiki/Arkady_and_Boris_Strugatsky" title="Arkady and Boris Strugatsky">Arkady and Boris Strugatsky</a>, who explored darker themes and social satire in their <a href="/wiki/Noon_Universe" title="Noon Universe">Noon Universe</a> novels, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_to_be_a_God" title="Hard to be a God">Hard to be a God</a></i> (1964) and <i><a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_Power" title="Prisoners of Power">Prisoners of Power</a></i> (1969), as well as in their <a href="/wiki/Science_fantasy" title="Science fantasy">science fantasy</a> trilogy <i><a href="/wiki/Monday_Begins_on_Saturday" title="Monday Begins on Saturday">Monday Begins on Saturday</a></i> (1964). A good share of Soviet science fiction was aimed at children. Probably the best known<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HD_SF_Lit_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-HD_SF_Lit-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kir_Bulychov" title="Kir Bulychov">Kir Bulychov</a>, who created <i><a href="/wiki/Alisa_Selezneva" title="Alisa Selezneva">Alisa Selezneva</a></i> (1965-2003), a children's space adventure series about a teenage girl from the future.</p>
<p>The Soviet film industry also contributed to the genre, starting from the 1924 film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aelita_(film)" title="Aelita (film)">Aelita</a></i>. Some of early Soviet films, namely <i><a href="/wiki/Planeta_Bur" title="Planeta Bur">Planet of the Storms</a></i> (1962) and <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_Beyond_the_Sun" title="Battle Beyond the Sun">Battle Beyond the Sun</a></i> (1959), were pirated, re-edited and released in the West under new titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Late Soviet science fiction films include <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mystery_of_the_Third_Planet" title="Mystery of the Third Planet">Mystery of the Third Planet</a></i> (1981), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ivan_Vasilyevich:_Back_to_the_Future" title="Ivan Vasilyevich: Back to the Future">Ivan Vasilyevich</a></i> (1973) and <i><a href="/wiki/Kin-dza-dza!" title="Kin-dza-dza!">Kin-dza-dza!</a></i> (1986), as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrey_Tarkovsky" title="Andrey Tarkovsky">Andrey Tarkovsky</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Solaris_(1972_film)" title="Solaris (1972 film)">Solaris</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Stalker_(1979_film)" title="Stalker (1979 film)">Stalker</a></i>, among others.</p>
<p>After the fall of the Soviet Union, science fiction in the former Soviet republics is still written mostly in <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, which allows an appeal to a broader audience. Aside from Russians themselves, especially notable are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_literature_in_Ukraine" title="Russian literature in Ukraine">Ukrainian writers</a>, who have greatly contributed to science fiction and fantasy in Russian language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pentacle_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-pentacle-129">[129]</a></sup> Among the most notable post-Soviet authors are <a href="/wiki/H._L._Oldie" title="H. L. Oldie">H. L. Oldie</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergey_Lukyanenko" title="Sergey Lukyanenko">Sergey Lukyanenko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Zorich" title="Alexander Zorich">Alexander Zorich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vadim_Panov" title="Vadim Panov">Vadim Panov</a>. Russia's film industry, however, has been less successful recently, producing only a few science fiction films, most of them are adaptations of books by the Strugatskys (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Inhabited_Island" title="The Inhabited Island">The Inhabited Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Ugly_Swans_(film)" title="The Ugly Swans (film)">The Ugly Swans</a></i>) or Bulychov (<i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Birthday" title="Alice's Birthday">Alice's Birthday</a></i>). Science fiction media in Russia is represented with such magazines as <i><a href="/wiki/Mir_Fantastiki" title="Mir Fantastiki">Mir Fantastiki</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Esli" title="Esli">Esli</a></i>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_European_countries">Other European countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Other European countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> is a traditional producer of science fiction and fantasy. The country's most influential science fiction writer of all time is <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Lem" title="Stanisław Lem">Stanisław Lem</a>, who is probably best known for his science fiction books, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Solaris_(novel)" title="Solaris (novel)">Solaris</a></i> and the stories involving <i><a href="/wiki/Ijon_Tichy" title="Ijon Tichy">Ijon Tichy</a></i>, but who also wrote very successful hard sci-fi such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Invincible" title="The Invincible">The Invincible</a></i> and the stories involving <a href="/wiki/Pilot_Pirx" title="Pilot Pirx">Pilot Pirx</a>. A number of Lem's books were adapted for screen, both in Poland and abroad. Other notable Polish writers of the genre include <a href="/wiki/Jerzy_%C5%BBu%C5%82awski" title="Jerzy Żuławski">Jerzy Żuławski</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janusz_A._Zajdel" title="Janusz A. Zajdel">Janusz A. Zajdel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Fia%C5%82kowski" title="Konrad Fiałkowski">Konrad Fiałkowski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacek_Dukaj" title="Jacek Dukaj">Jacek Dukaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rafa%C5%82_A._Ziemkiewicz" title="Rafał A. Ziemkiewicz">Rafał A. Ziemkiewicz</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech</a> writer and playwright <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a> in his play <i><a href="/wiki/R.U.R." title="R.U.R.">R.U.R.</a></i> (1920) introduced the word <i><a href="/wiki/Robot" title="Robot">robot</a></i> into science fiction. Čapek is also known for his satirical science fiction novels and plays, such as <i><a href="/wiki/War_with_the_Newts" title="War with the Newts">War with the Newts</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Absolute_at_Large" title="The Absolute at Large">The Absolute at Large</a></i>. Traditions of Czech science fiction were carried on despite the general political climate by writers like <a href="/wiki/Ludv%C3%ADk_Sou%C4%8Dek" title="Ludvík Souček">Ludvík Souček</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josef_Nesvadba" title="Josef Nesvadba">Josef Nesvadba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ond%C5%99ej_Neff" title="Ondřej Neff">Ondřej Neff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jaroslav_Velinsk%C3%BD" title="Jaroslav Velinský">Jaroslav Velinský</a>. In the years 1980 - 2000 a new vawe of young writers appeared (J. W. Procházka, F. Novotný, E. Hauserová, V. Kadlečková, J. Rečková, E. Dufková).</p>
<p>Early writers of <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslav</a> science fiction were mid-19th century <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovene</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Simon_Jenko" title="Simon Jenko">Simon Jenko</a>, late 19th century Slovene writers <a href="/wiki/Josip_Stritar" title="Josip Stritar">Josip Stritar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janez_Trdina" title="Janez Trdina">Janez Trdina</a> and Janez Mencinger (who, in 1893, published a notable dystopian novel <i><a href="/wiki/Abadon_(novel)" title="Abadon (novel)">Abadon</a></i>) and late 19th century <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbian</a> writers Dragutin Ilić and Lazar Komarčić. Since the beginning of the 20th century, a large number of authors incorporated science fiction elements into their work. Scientist <a href="/wiki/Milutin_Milankovi%C4%87" title="Milutin Milanković">Milutin Milanković</a> wrote the book <i><a href="/wiki/Through_Distant_Worlds_and_Times" title="Through Distant Worlds and Times">Through Distant Worlds and Times</a></i> (1928), which mixes elements of <a href="/wiki/Autobiography" title="Autobiography">autobiography</a>, scientific discussion and science fiction. In the 1930s, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Bartol" title="Vladimir Bartol">Vladimir Bartol</a> wrote a series of science fiction novels. The period after World War II brought the appearance of a large number of writers, most notably the duo Zvonimir Furtinger and Mladen Bjažić, <a href="/wiki/Vid_Pe%C4%8Djak" title="Vid Pečjak">Vid Pečjak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miha_Remec" title="Miha Remec">Miha Remec</a>, with some academically acclaimed writers, like <a href="/wiki/Dobrica_%C4%86osi%C4%87" title="Dobrica Ćosić">Dobrica Ćosić</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erih_Ko%C5%A1" title="Erih Koš">Erih Koš</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Ivanji" title="Ivan Ivanji">Ivan Ivanji</a>, occasionally turning towards science fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Serbian writer <a href="/wiki/Borislav_Peki%C4%87" title="Borislav Pekić">Borislav Pekić</a> published several science fiction works: <i>Rabies</i> (1983), <i>1999</i> (1984), <i>The New Jerusalem</i> (1988) and <i>Atlantis</i> (1988). <a href="/wiki/Zoran_%C5%BDivkovi%C4%87_(writer)" title="Zoran Živković (writer)">Zoran Živković</a> wrote a large number of essays on science fiction and one of the first encyclopedias of science fiction in the world. His early novels and stories featured elements of the genre. The films <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rat_Savior" title="The Rat Savior">The Rat Savior</a></i> (1977) by <a href="/wiki/Krsto_Papi%C4%87" title="Krsto Papić">Krsto Papić</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Visitors_from_the_Galaxy" title="Visitors from the Galaxy">Visitors from the Galaxy</a></i> (1981) by <a href="/wiki/Du%C5%A1an_Vukoti%C4%87" title="Dušan Vukotić">Dušan Vukotić</a> won awards at international festivals. In the first half of the 20th century comic book authors such as <a href="/wiki/Andrija_Maurovi%C4%87" title="Andrija Maurović">Andrija Maurović</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%90or%C4%91e_Loba%C4%8Dev" title="Đorđe Lobačev">Đorđe Lobačev</a> published a number of science fiction works, and since the 1980s comic book artists like <a href="/wiki/%C5%BDeljko_Pahek" title="Željko Pahek">Željko Pahek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Igor_Kordej" title="Igor Kordej">Igor Kordej</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoran_Janjetov" title="Zoran Janjetov">Zoran Janjetov</a> became internationally well-known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_fiction_in_Australia" title="Science fiction in Australia">Science fiction in Australia</a></div>
<p><b>Australia</b>: American <a href="/wiki/David_G._Hartwell" title="David G. Hartwell">David G. Hartwell</a> noted there is "nothing essentially Australian about Australian science-fiction." A number of Australian science-fiction (and fantasy and horror) writers are in fact international English language writers, and their work is published worldwide. This is further explainable by the fact that the Australian inner market is small (with Australian population being around 24 million), and sales abroad are crucial to most Australian writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_science_fiction" title="Canadian science fiction">Canadian science fiction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_science_fiction" title="American science fiction">American science fiction</a></div>
<p>In Canadian Francophone province <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Québec">Québec</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lisabeth_Vonarburg" title="Élisabeth Vonarburg">Élisabeth Vonarburg</a> and other authors developed a tradition of French-Canadian SF, related to the European French literature. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prix_Boreal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prix Boreal (page does not exist)">Prix Boreal</a> was established in 1979 to honor Canadian science fiction works in French. The <a href="/wiki/Prix_Aurora_Awards" title="Prix Aurora Awards">Prix Aurora Awards</a> (briefly preceded by the Casper Award) were founded in 1980 to recognize and promote the best works of Canadian science fiction in both French and English. Also, due to Canada's bilingualism and the US publishing almost exclusively in English, translation of science fiction prose into French thrives and runs nearly parallel upon a book's publishing in the original English. A sizeable market also exists within Québec for European-written Francophone science fiction literature.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_science_fiction" title="Brazilian science fiction">Brazilian science fiction</a></div>
<p>Although there is still some controversy as to when science fiction began in Latin America, the earliest works date from the late 19th century. All published in 1875, <i>O Doutor Benignus</i> by the Portuguese Brazilian <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Augusto_Em%C3%ADlio_Zaluar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Augusto Emílio Zaluar (page does not exist)">Augusto Emílio Zaluar</a>, <i>El Maravilloso Viaje del Sr. Nic-Nac</i> by the Argentinian Eduardo Holmberg, and <i>Historia de un Muerto</i> by the Cuban Francisco Calcagno are three of the earliest novels which appeared in the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balan.C3.A7o_2009:_Primeiros_romances_de_fic.C3.A7.C3.A3o_cient.C3.ADfica_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balan.C3.A7o_2009:_Primeiros_romances_de_fic.C3.A7.C3.A3o_cient.C3.ADfica-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Up to the 1960s, science fiction was the work of isolated writers who did not identify themselves with the genre, but rather used its elements to criticize society, promote their own agendas or tap into the public's interest in pseudo-sciences. It received a boost of respectability after authors such as <a href="/wiki/Horacio_Quiroga" title="Horacio Quiroga">Horacio Quiroga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a> used its elements in their writings. This, in turn, led to the permanent emergence of science fiction in the 1960s and mid-1970s, notably in Argentina, Brazil, Chile, Mexico, and Cuba. <a href="/wiki/Magic_realism" title="Magic realism">Magic realism</a> enjoyed parallel growth in Latin America, with a strong regional emphasis on using the form to comment on social issues, similar to social science fiction and speculative fiction in the English world.</p>
<p>Economic turmoil and the suspicious eye of the dictatorial regimes in place reduced the genre's dynamism for the following decade. In the mid-1980s, it became increasingly popular once more. Although led by Argentina, Brazil and Mexico, Latin America now hosts dedicated communities and writers with an increasing use of regional elements to set them apart from English-language science-fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reawakenings_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-reawakenings-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Science_fiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-pentacle-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-pentacle_129-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Oldie, H.L.; Dyachenko, Marina and Sergey; Valentinov, Andrey (2005). <bdi lang="ru">Пять авторов в поисках ответа (послесловие к роману "Пентакль")</bdi> [<i>Five authors in search for answers (an afterword to Pentacle)</i>] (in Russian). Moscow: Eksmo. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/5-699-09313-3" title="Special:BookSources/5-699-09313-3">5-699-09313-3</a>. <q>Украиноязычная фантастика переживает сейчас не лучшие дни. ... Если же говорить о фантастике, написанной гражданами Украины в целом, независимо от языка (в основном, естественно, на русском), — то здесь картина куда более радужная. В Украине сейчас работают более тридцати активно издающихся писателей-фантастов, у кого регулярно выходят книги (в основном, в России), кто пользуется заслуженной любовью читателей; многие из них являются лауреатами ряда престижных литературных премий, в том числе и международных.<br/>
<br/>
Speculative fiction in Ukrainian is living through a hard time today... Speaking of fiction written by Ukrainian citizens, regardless of language (primarily Russian, of course), there's a brighter picture. More than 30 fantasy and science fiction writers are active here, their books are regularly published (in Russia, mostly), they enjoy the readers' love they deserve; many are recipients of prestigious literary awards, including international.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AScience+fiction&amp;rft.au=Dyachenko%2C+Marina+and+Sergey&amp;rft.aufirst=H.L.&amp;rft.aulast=Oldie&amp;rft.au=Valentinov%2C+Andrey&amp;rft.btitle=%D0%9F%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8C+%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%D0%BE%D0%B2+%D0%B2+%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%85+%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0+%28%D0%BF%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%B5+%D0%BA+%D1%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83+%22%D0%9F%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%BB%D1%8C%22%29&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=5-699-09313-3&amp;rft.place=Moscow&amp;rft.pub=Eksmo&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
